O, my children, now I have completed this book for you, which is the last of the medical sciences. By its completion, by what it contains, and by its exposition and clarification, in my view I have explained to those who love me this treatise, which is part of what I do with my hands, namely surgery.
For surgical practice has been completely abandoned in our region and in our time, except that the science of it is rare, and its remnants remain; and there are no descriptions of it other than small ones in the books of the Ancients, which have altered much of what they contain, leading to errors and hesitation, because they obscure the intentions of surgery and weaken its power and art.
And it seems to me that I should revive this treatise according to the order of exposition, explanation, and abbreviation; and so that it may come with the forms of cautery tools and other instruments for the work as it is through the addition of explanations, and their preparation, which cause is necessary.
But the reason why a good craftsman is not found in our time in this field of surgery is that medical science is extensive, and it requires the practitioner to be well-versed in anatomy, which involves knowing the functions of the members and their forms, as well as their conjunctions and separations; and to have knowledge of bones, nerves, and muscles, along with their numbers and distribution; and of pulsating veins and static veins, and the locations for dissecting them.
And for this reason Hippocrates said that in name there is much, but in the work there is little, especially in the art of the hand.
This may we have said already at the beginning of this book; for whoever is not aware of what we have said about anatomy cannot be exempt from falling into error through which men are rendered dead, as I have seen too many of them, for some are formed in this science and throw themselves into it without that knowledge and experience.
For I saw a mad physician who had incised an abscess in the neck of a woman and cut some arteries of the neck, so that much blood flowed out causing the woman to die immediately.
And I saw another physician who thought he would remove a stone from a man who was ill in his state, and the stone was large, and when he came to him he extracted that stone along with a piece of the bladder, so that on the third day that man died.
And I had already realized that it was impossible to remove that stone, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I knew that he would die.
And I saw another physician who was receiving payment from a duke of our land to perform surgery, and he attended to a black castrated man who had a fracture in his skull; due to the physician's ignorance, he exposed the fracture over the wound with bandages and strong binding, but did not leave room for drainage.
And shortly after he loosened it according to his desires, and then he dressed it again, and afterwards he left it like that for several days and commanded us to remove the bandage; and thus it remained until the skull and the bone became abscessed, leading to his ruin.
Thus he calls to him, and prepares to loosen the ligature, and having obtained calm he begins to relieve his pains.
But when corruption was confirmed in the limb, so that it could not be restrained, and the corruption continued to spread throughout the limb until danger threatened the patient.
And I saw another surgeon who punctured a cancerous abscess, and it became ulcerated near the thighs, until the malignancy spread throughout the body of his lord.
The reason for this is that cancerous tumors are free of melancholic humor; therefore, it does not need to affect the whole body, but rather can be in a limb which suffers from being fully affected.
And for this reason, my children, it is necessary for you that the operation be planned in two aspects: one is an operation associated with health, and the other is work in which there is risk even under the best conditions.
And I have already expressed this in every part of this book where an operation involves both error and fear; therefore, it is necessary for you to heed this and avoid that, so that your hands do not find a way to speak against you and dishonor you.
And so take up your arms with diligence and the protection of God, and treat your patients with skill and firmness.
And use the best path by God's grace to lead to health and praiseworthy success, and avoid terrible diseases that are difficult to heal, and remain armed against what causes you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and reputation does not betray you: for it is better for your glory, and more in this world and at last for your sanctity.
Wine should be used in some surgical procedures: Do not treat illness badly, for then bad doctors would not be named.
And thus I have dedicated this book in three parts.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter is ordered on the head inside-out, in the forms of the instruments and in the tools of the cauteries, and of what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, on cutting and puncturing, and on cupping, and on plasters, and on the extraction of arrows and similar items, all chaptered and ordered in the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, on restoration and dislocation, and on the treatment of fractures and similar conditions; a chapter ordered concerning the head inside-out, and in the forms of the instruments.
I shall speak of the quality of the movements of that cautery and of its effects, and in what condition cautery is prohibited.
Therefore, I say that the speech about the quality of the movement of cautery and its effects is long and extensive, subtle by science, and hidden as a secret; many wise people have spoken about it, and they disagree on it. But for me, a little of their speeches suffices to avoid prolongation.
Therefore, I say that cauteries apply universally to all complete structures, whether they have material or not, with the exception of two structures, which are tailless structures.
In the tail and dry structure that has material, it dries out; for some people say that cauterization is helpful for this, while others say the opposite, that cauterization is not appropriate in a disease caused by heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is hot and dry, and it is not beneficial to treat a tail and dry condition with something that is hot and dry.
And those who say the opposite are mistaken, that cauterization is beneficial for every tail and dry disease that occurs in human bodies, because when you compare the body of a person and its humidity to the nature of fire, you find that the human body is cold.
And I say according to the words of that person, that experience has already shown this to me on several occasions.
But I do not intend to go into detail about that for one who has already been instructed and trained in the chapter on cauterization through previous exercise, and who knows the diversity of human structure and the disposition of diseases in their seasons, as well as the causes of them and their accidents, and the time span of these.
But in other structures, there is no need for concern, especially in cold and humid diseases, because all doctors are now in agreement about those, and they do not disagree on the use of cauterization in such cases.
And you know, my children, that the secret of healing through cauterization with fire and its effectiveness lies in the fact that fire is simple, and its action affects only the member being cauterized and does not harm other parts adjacent to it except slightly.
Here is the English translation:
Take the fire, by its sublimity and goodness of its substance, it does nothing but superfluify. And it is known to us through experiment and length of service in the study of the art about the truth of the causes; and thereby I excuse myself from the length of speech. And if not that it were read in my book at this place I hide secret with the fire, and the quality of its operation on the bodies and expulsion thereof by which you expel diseases, in philosophical demonstrative speech, would be acuity in your heads.
And know, children, that they disagree about the time when it is convenient to perform cautery and they pause at the best time of spring.
And I say that cautery is convenient at all times, because the harm caused by accidental time is submerged in the benefit of what is attracted through cautery, and even more so if the cautery is for pains or necessary pain, strong and urgent, which cannot tolerate delay due to fear of those things that may cause malice, which is worse than a small harm entering through an inconvenient time.
And this does not occur in your minds, or, children, it is only valued by the common people and foolish doctors that for the illness which is cured by cautery, it is not always opportune, and they pause at that moment.
And the reason is not as such because they think about it, for cautery is nothing but a substitute for medicine that alters the complexion and dries the humidity which is the cause of pains.
But nevertheless, cautery surpasses medicine in its efficacy and the virtue of its operation and the greatness of its power.
It is possible that his illness may return at some time according to the condition of the patient and the location of the illness, and its nature, and what has been prepared in his body from the accumulation of excesses within him, and what he acquires through food and similar causes; but if the disease for which cauterization is administered is a subtle one with little excess and moisture, such as tooth cauterization for pain and similar cases, it is possible that the pain will not return in him, although this applies to minor instances.
And the common saying is that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine, and this is said by barbers, not by those who understand it, for I believe that there is no cure gained through medicine or any other cause before cauterization.
And the cause is contrary to that; and the understanding that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine is only when we administer methods of treatment for certain illnesses, and those medicines are ineffective, and at the end of the cause, cauterization is administered, and it proves effective in such cases. And here from what was said before that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine, not according to the understanding which I understand of the common and excesses of foolish physicians.
And the ancients said that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they did not say this except for the equality of gold and the sublimity of its substance.
And they said that the place for cauterization does not matter, which is not absolutely true, because I have found it proven and corrected that this applies only to certain bodies and not others, and cauterization with gold is good and better than iron as they say; but when you make cauterization with fire using gold, it is not clear to you, for it depends on the quantity you want due to the hardness of gold, and it prepares to receive friction; and if you adjust over it with fire, the gold melts by the heat, and placing some sheets in the fire allows the craftsman to shape it according to that.
And thus with water is made the cautery of iron; you should remove the pus lightly and properly according to the correctness of the procedure.
And I have already arranged this chapter on cautery according to the chapters, and I have arranged those of the head among the more serious cases, so that I alleviate the seeker who asks for what he wants from it.
The first chapter.
This cautery is applied to the cold and moisture that cause headaches and two other conditions resulting from these causes: one is caused by soda and many cases of traumatic flux, called herpes, affecting the head and parts of the ears with fluid discharge, and many cases of sleepiness, toothache, and gullet pain, and generally all diseases that come from coldness, such as paralysis, stroke, and similar conditions.
The form of this cautery is as follows: first, command the patient to evacuate with a laxative medicine that cleanses the head for three or four nights, according to what you require based on the strength of the patient and their age and usual habits.
And then command him to shave his head with a razor, and then place him sitting between the sheets on a padded bench and have him rest his head on his hands.
And then place the razor in your palm near the spot above the nose between the eyebrows. But when the temperature of your left hand reaches it, mark that spot with ink, and then apply the olive oil cautery, which has this form:
And after pausing on that spot marked with a tent, in the manner which descends, with which the skin may be gradually stretched, and you turn it.
And then move your hand gently and look at the spot, and if you see any amount of bone already uncovered, as much as the amount of grain in one's palm, then raise your hand, and if not return your hand to that tool, or another like it, if it is cooled, until you can see the bone as I have told you.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it over the spot and let it be for three days.
And then pause on the embedded knot at the border and let it be inside until the burn scar is destroyed. And then treat the spot with a quadrature ointment until it is healed.
And it is said that when the wound has become completely covered with new flesh, the sooner the bandage is removed, the better and more helpful it is.
And some of them say that the wound should be cauterized down to the bone, and the cautery should be held until it adheres to some part of the bone.
And then rasp what is adhered to the bone, and then treat it.
And others say that with the cautery reach to the deepest part, until it presses firmly on the bone until the bone melts, and this is the method of alpeccati (cauterizing slightly): And they believe that from that spot emanate the vapors of the head.
And let the wound be treated for a long time until it is healed and has begun to heal properly.
But I have not seen these two species of cautery used on everyone in the same way out of fear; and leave that one, as I believe, it is better for health when it is so; for the head is easily injured by a solution of continuity just like we have testified with other members, and especially when the patient's constitution is cold.
And the first species of cautery is safer and better, in my opinion; and from that one use it for the sake of health.
Chapter Second.
Of cautery of the head.
When chronic and long-lasting pain occurs throughout the head and this is prolonged in the patient, and you use plasters, poultices, caps, ointments, compresses, cataplasms, and similar treatments, especially if it has already been cauterized with a cautery that I mentioned but to no avail for any of those causes, then consider whether the patient's head is of strong constitution in its nature; and if it is not cold and you find significant inflammation, then at that time cauterize with another type of cautery after that one. And then cauterize it above the crown of their head with cautery until the pain is relieved. Cut open the area of pain and expose the bone in the quantity that we have described, and cauterize it with a cautery at the back of the head in the place called "vas," — which is the location where the temporal vein is situated. And do this with your hand in this way, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will experience great pain from it different from the pain of all other cauteries of the head.
And I will describe this cautery in its place, for it is more delicate than the one used to cauterize the horns of the head and the occiput; and this is its form.
Chapter Three.
On Cauterization of Recent Wounds.
When severe pain is located in the forehead or persistent pain within the head occurs, it should be treated with medicines that cleanse the head, and other treatments should be administered as I have described for various illnesses. And if this does not help, then cauterization can be performed in two ways: either using a sharp burning medicine or with iron.
But if you use a burning medicine for the cautery, then take a piece of dough made from alum, dry it thoroughly, and remove both ends of it. And then you should incise the area of the pain on the temple with a lancet slightly below the hairline so that it is hidden.
And then apply pressure to it with bandages for continuous compression for three hours; then loosen them and remove the alum, and leave the wound covered for two or three days. After that, place a poultice soaked in butter on it until the area becomes warm.
And then care for it with remedies until it is healed.
And if you wish, use those medicines which I have written in the second treatise on burning medicines.
And this is how you perform cautery with iron using this cautery tool, which has the following form:
It is called a key clamp because the head of it resembles the shape of a nail head, which has some curvature, and in the middle part of it there is a small prominence.
And then place it on the location of the pain and hold your hand — so that the artery below is not adhered to — and move the iron slightly back and forth quickly.
And if the amount that is adhered due to the curvature is as much as half of it, raise your hand so that the artery below is not adhered, so that there is no bleeding.
And then soak cotton in salt water and place it on the location, and leave it for three days; and afterward apply honey around it, and subsequently treat it with an ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, perform cautery at the end of the radius according to the cautery method, and be careful not to cut the artery in this non-traditional procedure.
Chapter four.
On the cautery of an ancient incision.
When you treat an incision with what we have said about the previous treatment and the divisions of diseases, and if it does not improve, and sometimes due to the disease it is insufficient for what we have mentioned about the first cautery with medicine or fire cautery, then you should ignite the radial cautery until it becomes white-hot. Place it on the site of the pain in a line whose length is half the width of your finger or slightly more; then lift your hand quickly and press it strongly so that it cuts into the artery but does not reach the bone.
However, you must guard against the continuation of the jawbone, which is necessary for chewing, and ensure that the muscle is not adhered and the nerves which animate it are not damaged, so that spasm does not occur.
And if it is about the care and caution of the flow of blood from the artery that is being tied off, because there is fear in doing so, especially for one who does not know what they are doing and is not skilled and experienced; and leave the operation at that point at the beginning.
And here I will come to the recollection of the management of blood flow from an artery according to the usual method, in its place in this book.
And if you see that this disease does not respond to these cauteries and the patient's condition is tolerable, cauterize in the appropriate place of the head as we have recounted, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you perform the cauteries that we have mentioned in the chapter on the rupture of an artery with a cautery having two prongs, this is a better and more effective cautery.
Chapter 5.
On the cautery for ear pains.
When pain in the ear occurs due to friction and is treated with laxative causes and with the other treatment that I have mentioned in the division, and if the pain does not improve, now use the cautery, which is called puncta. This is its form:
And approach the puncta to the fire, and move it around the entire ear as a wheel revolves, or around each part if the pain is in that part. Hold the cautery on the rim of the ear little by little until you have marked the place with it.
And if you apply the cautery at these points throughout the ear, and wherever it is needed, and then treat the areas until they heal.
Chapter 6.
On the cautery of the throat.
The throat that is cured by cautery and not through medication is caused solely by phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cautery for the type that results from dryness or spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of throat condition with herbs and purgatives and other remedies, but your treatment does not provide relief, then it is necessary for you to cauterize the illness in three places: the first at the rim of the ear, the second slightly below the temple, and the third at the junction of the two lips.
And pause your cautery at the opposite side of the affected area, for relief does not come from the part that is diseased but from the surrounding healthy tissue.
The method of cautery is that you cauterize the affected area with the cautery at the right extremity of the ear slightly above the horn of the head, and at the temple in another place, the length of which should be according to the length of a thumb, and then pause your hand with the cautery until half the quantity of the condition is consumed.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is specific to the surgeon, whose form is laid out above. But more subtle is that which you see in a small amount; and it is necessary to use the knife instead of excessive coarseness in a small amount.
And properly care for the place with water as it was previously treated until it is healed.
Chapter VII.
On the cautery for chronic apoplexy.
When apoplexy is chronic and you treat it with water as we have said and your medication does not cause fever in the patient, then cauterize it with four cauteries: above all the temples of the head; and at a cauterium and the middle part of the head, as we have said; and at a cauterium in the nape of the neck. And the method of the cautery is done as was said above.
And it has already been cauterized over the mouth of the stomach; and it is better that way; and now it should be treated with water as you have prescribed.
Chapter VIII.
On the cautery for forgetfulness caused by phlegm.
It is necessary to give the patient large doses of decoctions and cleansing pills at the beginning.
And then shave the patient's head completely and apply a plaster to the nape of the neck using the one described in the chapter on plasters.
And pause at certain points, for that is the method of cauterization. And do this according to the order which I have already mentioned.
And thus if it heals well; but otherwise, at that time perform the three cauterizations on the rear part of his head, which descend from the most superior part of the head to the part nearest the neck. And pause between each cauterization for the thickness of your finger, and then proceed with the next cauterization.
But if you prefer and the condition allows it, then perform the cauterization in the middle location; and if you want a stronger effect, then cauterize above both horns.
Then proceed to ensure it heals; and let the cauterizations be olive-shaped according to the form given before.
Chapter IX.
On the cauterization of parasites and the softening of the entire body.
Proceed with the cleansing of the head using scissors and as we have said; then shave the patient's head, and subsequently cauterize it in the middle part of the head, above all the horns, and on the rear part of the head, and with three cauterizations along the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary for the condition of softening the body to do so and the patient can tolerate it, especially when the illness is severe, then cauterize with four cauterizations along the vertebrae of the back, and finally perform one cauterization near the navel area, raising the skin slightly. For one side there is treatment that heals, and for this other there is olive oil cauterization.
Chapter 10.
On the cautery for epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized unless the epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And therefore, one should modify the brain of the patient at the beginning of the treatment and apply the other cure that we have mentioned in the division when the patient is severe and tolerant of the exception of the medicines.
And if it is a child who cannot tolerate the medicines, then administer sweets and masticatory substances that modify the brain, before which for several days, improve the quality of the food.
And then shave the patient's head, and subsequently cauterize one area at the middle of the head according to the previous method, and another cauterization at the occiput, and on each side of the head as well. And if the patient is strong and tolerant, then cauterize as I have mentioned, having paralysis and relaxation of the body on the neck vertebrae and the thoracic vertebrae.
And it should be olive oil cauterization as was previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then use gentle cauterization according to this method.
Chapter 11.
On the cautery for melancholia.
When the causes of melancholia are corrupted humidity and thick phlegm, then cauterize that area with the cauteries which we have mentioned in the case of paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholia is excess declining to melancholia, and the body of the patient is humid, then give him to drink that which purifies the brain as has been mentioned previously in the division.
And then shave the patient's head, and then place a well-made poultice over the circle, and then apply that to the middle of the head.
And the patient should be seated, and held from all sides.
And then apply a liniment; it is more suitable to use one made from sheep's wool. And warm that on the fire with moderate heat, as it should be endured when applied. And then place that in the middle of the head within the circle, and let it be rubbed in.
And do this for the patient once a week according to their regimen until they are healed; or if you prefer, cauterize that with small points as needed, but use your hand with a cautery so arranged: this is a type of cautery that moistens the brain with equilibrium.
And then apply that lotion infused in wool or in linen cloths.
Chapter XII.
On the cauterization of water descending in the belly.
When you have determined the beginning of the water in the wound by the signs which I have mentioned in the division, then give the sick person to drink that which cleanses the head from all humidity.
And make him sweat in baths for several days.
And then command him to shave his head, and cauterize that area with a cautery in the middle of the head; and then cauterize that area with two cauteries on two temples if it is the beginning of water formation in two wounds, or from one side if it is the beginning of water formation in one wound.
And with the cautery cut open all the veins and arteries that lead to it; and place the cauteries at a distance in the latitude of the temples.
And guard against bleeding when it is cauterized; and if you see the patient bleeding, stop it with some medicine that is available to you.
And I will come to the knowledge that is in the extraction of arteries and the treatment to prevent bleeding.
And sometimes it is cauterized at the neck behind two bones with two cauteries produced in that manner.
Chapter XIII.
On the Cauterization of Old Abscesses.
When the abscesses of the wounds have become old and persistently remain due to veins and arteries that are evident in the visible part of the head, and it is confirmed that this is cold phlegmatic excess, then cauterize that area with the cautery which we have described in the chapter on the beginning of water accumulation in wounds, and with a cautery at the middle part of the head, and with two cauteries behind the two temples, and with two cauteries behind two bones at the neck. And if you need an addition, then cauterize that at the entire base of the nail on the extremity of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter xiiii.
On Cauterization for Nasal Congestion.
When you treat that person with what we have mentioned in the division and they do not benefit from your treatment, then prepare and give the sick person cow's bile to drink for three nights; afterwards, shave their head, and cauterize them with a medium-sized cautery using olive oil. And then cauterize that person with a small rose-shaped cautery above the two eyebrows, just over the hair, slightly.
And be careful not to burn that area.
And this is the form of the small rose-shaped cautery.
Chapter .xv.
On Cauterization for Softening the Eyelids.
When the eyelid is softened due to illness or moisture, then cauterize that eyelid with this lunar-shaped cautery; and this is the form of it:
And if you do so, cauterize that area above the two eyebrows slightly with two cauteries in total, and let them be elongated towards the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the quantity of the eyebrow.
Do not press too hard with your tool when cauterizing, but rather to the extent that one third of the eyelid is adhered.
Chapter XVI.
About cauterizing the eyelid when the hairs have turned inward and are touching the eye.
When the hairs of the eye have turned inward and are touching the eye, you should cauterize in two ways: either by direct application or as part of a burning medicine.
If you cauterize with fire, instruct the patient to trim their hairs as do those who are clean-shaven, making them longer and even; and if they irritate the area of growth, then wrap the eyes in a bandage so that nothing enters until the hairs grow back.
Once they have grown back and are even, then instruct the patient to place their head in your lap, and gently apply a small bandage or patch over the eyelids where the cauterization was done, marking it in the shape of a leaf, with the beginning of the mark near the hairs.
And gently place a piece of cotton in the eye or apply a mucilage of feathers on the eye.
And thus the cauterization should be performed according to this method.
And then cauterize over the marked figure little by little, in small stages, until the surface of the area shaped like a myrtle leaf is completely burned through.
A sign of health in this work is that you see the eyelid already contracted and the hair on it raised above the puncture of the eye.
And then raise your hand and wash it for three days, and afterwards place a bandage on it until the scar is healed. And then take care of that with herbs until it is healed.
But if the hair returns for any reason and the eyelid becomes softened, then perform the cautery again on that area as you did initially.
But if the hair remains in the eyelid, then cauterize that area until it is raised to its natural position and healed so that the hairs do not touch the eye.
But the cautery and burning medicine should be such that you instruct the patient to pluck the hairs until they are long enough and healed, and then make from paper or cloth the shape of a myrtle leaf.
And then take known soap and one ounce of live lard, or something similar, and mix these ingredients well, and apply it quickly so that it is not rubbed in; then spread this mixture over the paper made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place that on both eyes; and cover the eye with a cloth dipped in cold water, and have the patient's head covered as well.
And place your finger on the medicine and press it gently so that the patient feels the burning sensation of the medicine, for this burning is like that of fire.
And thus, when the biting has stopped, leave the medicine and wash the wound with water; then, once the biting has ceased, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; and now look to see if the eyelid is raised as it was washed. If so, loosen the constriction with fire or incision, for that is well; but if not, return to the application of the medicine in the place where you have not applied it until it is absorbed into your operation and the eye is constricted.
And then pause on the cut at the border until the mark of the treatment is eradicated, and then treat that with defined care or another method until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you to guard with vigilant diligence that no part of the medicine falls into the eye, and if the eyelid after some days becomes softened due to some cause of the hair root in the eye, then repeat your medicine on that place where the eyelid has become softened, and properly as you did at the beginning, and then wait until it is healed.
And this is the form of the leaf of myrtle.
And know that the eyes of people vary in size and smallness, and therefore according to this, your contraction is made.
And the way to practice this art will not be hidden from one who studies in this field.
Chapter XVII.
On the cautery treatment of a fistula in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula with water as we have said in the divisions of diseases and do not confer with your treatment, then it is necessary that you cauterize it according to this method: command the patient to place his head either on his hands or on your lap, and let another minister hold his head still in such a way that it is neither too tight nor agitated.
And then place cotton in the cavity or on the wound surface.
And then ignite the cautery, which has this form:
which is cone-shaped, like the shape of a reed or an eagle's feather, with one end where the cautery is made.
And if you wish, it can penetrate into the other end. And if you want it to be as thin as a thread.
But the one that is cone-shaped is better for your work.
And then press the fistula if it is open, and extract the pus and dry it out. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the putrefaction from it.
And then place the cautery, which should be well ignited, on it, and do this gently so that your hand does not shake or disturb the patient, and then place the cautery on the edges of the wound and burn it.
And if at the beginning of your cauterization it penetrates to the bone, that is good; and if not, then repeat the cauterization once or twice if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, and then place cotton around it with a drying agent applied until it is healed.
If after ten days it has not healed, then now apply acute corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed, according to what is convenient in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula which the ancients first described: insert into the location of the fistula and make that opening, then apply to that fissure finely powdered material of which the form is like this: And pour into it one ounce of melted lead; and with your finger carefully press so that there should not be much moisture lest the melted lead reach the flesh around it; and it must be applied over the wound wrapped in egg white or in water, for lead adheres to the place of the fistula and heals it miraculously.
And if the fistula is healed by what we have said about cauterization and treatment, that is good; but if not, it is now necessary to treat it through the nose and reduce the fistula to follow the course of the nose according to how it will come into its proper place.
Chapter XVIII.
On the cauterization of fissures of the teeth.
Fissures often occur in the lips, and especially in the lips of children.
Therefore, when you treat those fissures with what we have mentioned in the divisions and your treatment is not effective, now use small knife-shaped cautery according to this form that will be shown:

Chapter XIX.
On cauterizing a fistula in the mouth.

Chapter XX.
On cauterizing the teeth and on softening the gums.

Then take your hand, and then repeat the cauteries as many times as you wish.
Then the patient should rinse his mouth with salt water and keep doing this for one hour, after which stop it, for the rotten tooth is arrested and the softened gum is strengthened and the corrupted moisture is dried up.
Chapter XXI.
On the cautery for toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness, or if it is numb or green, and you do not wish to treat it with medicine, then the cautery in such a case is done in two ways: either by means of a cow's horn, or by fire.
Cautery with a horn is that you take a cow's horn and boil it in oil, then take some cotton and wrap it at the tip of the horn. Then immerse it in the boiled horn until it is soaked, and place it quickly on the painful tooth while still warm.
Then repeat this several times until the effect of the heat reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, soak wool or cotton in the aforementioned horn oil and place it on the painful tooth, then put hot iron on top of that until the heat reaches the source of the pain in the tooth.
However, the cautery with fire is that you attend to the cautery of iron or steel, and it should be in the middle so that the intensity of the heat does not reach the patient's mouth.
From the fire make the cautery of which I will describe the form, and place it on the tooth until the cautery is rubbed in; and do this several times so that the pain may be alleviated either on that day or the next.
And it happens that by pressure of that cautery the patient fills his mouth with good saliva and holds it for an hour, and then spits it out; and this is the form of the cautery.
And cauterize as much as you wish according to what is possible for you.

Chapter XXVIII.
On the Incision of an Abscess of the Testicle with Caustics.

And take care of that with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for forty days until the place is sealed and the wound healed.
And it is necessary to restrict the patient's diet according to the length of time required for their treatment, so that the intestines do not protrude due to the effort of healing.
And when it is appropriate to get up after forty days, administer care properly, and keep that person bedridden for another forty days.
And limit their work and the consumption of food and drink, and speaking loudly, for when they follow this complete regimen of healing, they will be healed.
And I will describe the treatment of ruptures in other parts of the abdomen. At the beginning, if you do not want them to spread, then cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to the size of it; remove as much as is taken from the cord, as one-third of it. And afterward treat that as we have said, and it will not be adjusted.
And some earlier practitioners have said that the rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery in this form, as it is marked with a template:
This is a paused line that you follow for the latitude in the skin above the rupture, and another line above it; then place your hand with a cautery nail, but the first cautery is farther and better.
Chapter XLVI.
On the cautery treatment for dislocations.
When pain in any member due to falling or striking persists for a long time and does not improve with medicine, it is then necessary to treat it with a pointed cautery.
And the size of the cautery for every member should be according to its size and smallness, and its strength and weakness, and the location of the dislocation and the pain in it.
If it heals with the first cautery, that is good; but if not, then apply the cautery again. For it is customary for such pains to be punctured in a member near where they are located, so you must achieve this with the cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter XLVII.
On the cautery treatment for leprosy.
Some cases of leprosy are greatly helped by cautery, but rarely when the type of leprosy is caused by the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And so when you wish to cauterize that patient, consider whether the leprosy is commencing and can be cured with what we have discussed in the division and do not delay. If you fear that the corruption will encompass the entire body of the patient and all its composition, then cauterize him on the head with five cauteries: one at the middle part of the head, and another above it near the forehead; two on both horns; and one behind, either on the nape or neck.
And last of all your cautery a little inside so as to press into the bone a small depression, and thereby displace the scabs, so that the exhalation of thick vapors may be alleviated; and cauterize over the mucus as is necessary.
And if the leprosy has already spread over the body and is clearly manifested, then you should cauterize in those places with those cauteries which we have mentioned at the beginning; and cauterize one on the tip of the nose, two on each cheek, two on the vertebrae of the neck, six on the back vertebrae, one large one near the buttocks, one on the buttock itself, two on each thigh, one on each shin, two on each joint, and one on each finger joint, two on each instep, and two on each muscle, and two on the soles of the feet; cauterize in all the joints of the fingers and toes, and on each heel of their feet, and on the hollows of their feet.
And be careful of the nerves which lie above the heel so as not to burn them.
And in some cases it is cauterized with one cautery on the bone of the little finger and another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the liver.
And know that the more you adjust the cauteries, the more beneficial and effective they are; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery as others would, because the body is already numb.
And it is appropriate that the cauteries be large or small according to the members and joints as required by the nature of the tools.
For the treatment of cautery, use flour of oats mixed with honey. And for the other cure, let it be made healthy.
Chapter XLVIII.
On cauterization for fainting.
When a limb is numb or asleep and is treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, but does not heal, then you should cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size of the limb or its smallness.
And let the heat of the cautery enter slightly into the depth of the skin. And subsequently treat that area with an ointment so that it may heal.
And sometimes fainting is cauterized in certain places that are near the ribs and to the chest and to the vertebrae of the back, and along the course of the nerves which move that limb, and thus the numbness is alleviated.
But do not proceed beyond this unless one is wise in the anatomy of limbs and the course of nerves throughout the body that move them.
Chapter XLIX.
On cauterization of ulcers that become infected.
When an old ulcer does not respond to any of the medicinal ointments, then you should cauterize it with a cautery slightly, according to the quantity and specificity of the depth of the skin, until the whiteness and color of it are alleviated, and its color changes.
For the treatment, mix flour of wheat and lentils with rose oil and arnica leaves, and add pigeon blood or swallow blood, taking an equal part of each and thoroughly mixing them together. Then apply a cloth soaked in this mixture to the affected area until it is healed.
Chapter 10
On the cautery treatment for cancer.
When cancer begins and you want to remove it, then cauterize that area with a circular cautery as it appears.
The wise ones say that it should be cauterized with the deepest cautery in the middle area; but I have not seen this done, for I fear that it may become ulcerated, and I have observed this happening several times.
It is safer to cauterize around it in a circular manner as we have said, or with multiple cauteries.
Chapter 11
On the cautery treatment for tumors.
When a tumor is delayed in maturation due to excess moisture in that area, or because of the patient's condition, being old and weak, with little blood, or due to time, and you want to hasten its maturation; then cauterize around it with many small punctures. After this, leave it be, as it will hasten towards maturation.
But if you want to perforate it with a cautery, then choose the appropriate cautery for this purpose. Place that cautery in the center of the tumor until it penetrates the tissue.
And if you apply cautery in that place where there is pus, so that the arm may be relieved; and then treat that part which needs to be healed.
Chapter LII.
On the Cauterization with Corrosive Substances.
A corrosive is a wandering corruption in the limb, which eats away just as fire consumes dry wood.
Therefore, when you see that corrosive in a place where it can be treated with fire, then you ignite cauterizations with small and large branding irons as is appropriate in the place where the corrosive is.
And then cauterize it thoroughly so that you eradicate all the corrosion and leave no cause for it to remain.
And then dress it for three days, and pause on the cauterized areas three times a day with oil mixed so that all scars are eradicated and all corruption is removed.
And then treat it with substances that promote flesh growth.
But if after three days you see that the flesh grows healthy with no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cautery on what remains in the corrupted areas.
And some corrosives are treated with sharp medicine, for that is the method in place of cautery. But cautery with fire is more quickly effective.
And I have already mentioned the treatment with sharp medicine in the division, and therefore use that when you find it appropriate for the condition.
Chapter LIII.
On the cautery of nails and other similar parts.
Often in the parts below the foot there is a malady called "claw," which is caused by thick and hard flesh that deforms the foot; and this condition can be treated by cautery in two ways, namely fire cautery and hot water cautery.
The fire cautery is performed by heating the cautery tool, which should be concave in shape and similar to a half-circle, made of iron according to the size that fits around the entire affected area, and it should have fine edges.
And then place the heated cautery on the affected nail, and immediately rotate your hand with the cautery around the nail until the cautery reaches the depth of the nail. And leave it for three days until it begins to scab over. And then apply a plaster made of crushed wild mullein and salt to it, and leave the plaster on for one night, for this will eradicate it from its roots.
And then treat the area with a generative flesh ointment until it is healed.
And if it does not convert, and often occurs on the surface of the body, particularly on the palms and feet, then you should administer hot water cautery to them. This is done by taking a small tube made of earthenware or iron or half-circle in shape, and place it over the affected nail; then pour a small amount of hot water into the tube and hold it with your hand, rotating it thoroughly so that the edge of the tube touches the root of the nail; as the hot water penetrates to the base of the nail, wait for the malady to be slightly burned by the hot water for about an hour, then remove it, for the nail along with its roots will be eradicated.
And thus place one part close to the other until you cover all of them as they naturally should be in the body.
And bring close the care of those abscesses when the roots of the nails are removed with the remedies that cause flesh to grow.
Chapter LIII.
On the cautery for tremors.
When you find a tremor due to irritation of the nerves or from fever causing irritation, or from some similar cause, it is then that you should cauterize it with four cauteries or five on the vertebrae of the back, between each vertebra one cauterization; and at the feet of him, and above his stomach, a cauterization in the shape of an olive.
And the tremor of him subsides and hastens the recovery from the sickly condition caused by irritation.
Chapter LIV.
On the cautery for accidental injuries to the body.
When in the body there appear accidental swellings and pus-filled wounds that are formed from thick, corrupted matter; therefore, it is necessary at the beginning when they appear, to cauterize over the entire head of the fistula with a fine cautery made of myrtle wood, the tip of which is extended over fire; or with the long edge of erestologia; or with a lens-shaped cautery.
And sometimes it is necessary to cauterize immediately at the beginning of its expulsion according to this method; and if not done so, the excess that causes them is disrupted, and they heal from those.
However, it must be done close to evacuation rather than phlebotomy.
Chapter LVI.
On cauterization of blood flow due to accidental cutting of an artery.
If the flow of blood from an artery that has been cut is due to a plague that occurred before, or due to the beginning of abscesses or cauterization of the limb, and similar cases, then it is difficult to remedy that.
When such an incident occurs, immediately place your thumb on the opening of the artery, and press over it until the bleeding stops and no more blood escapes.
And then place the olive-coated cauteries, both small and large, in the fire for a number of times until they are ignited.
And then take one of those cauteries, either small or large depending on the location and condition in which the artery is ruptured, and apply it quickly after you have stopped the bleeding with your thumb, so that the blood flow is sealed and restricted.
And if the blood is expelled from the opening of the artery when you remove your thumb and apply the cautery, then take another cautery immediately from those that are prepared in the fire, and continue doing this one after another until the bleeding is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is here, and do not cause any other harm.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, it is not possible to stop the blood at that moment, especially if the artery is large, unless one does one of four things: either use cauterization as we have described, or perform an extraction because when it is extracted, the extremities are burned and the blood is constricted, or bind it with a strong ligature or apply medicines whose virtue is to restrict the blood; and strengthen them with a pillow and proper constriction.
And let him who administers proper restraint with the bandage and constriction with cloths or by positioning burning substances and similar items not hurry but act slowly.
And if anyone attempts this and the physician is not present nor are medicines available, then open with care using the index finger on the opening of that vein as we have said, and understand the great constriction inside so that the blood stops from flowing from the main part of the wound, and over the artery let the finger not be moved; and above the artery sprinkle it with cold water to make the blood congeal and thicken; and then proceed with cauterization or necessary medicine.
On incisions and punctures and ventouses and phlebotomy and wounds and their similarities.
We have already discussed in the first chapter all maladies in which cautery with fire is appropriate and with burning medicine, and the occasions and causes of it, and the instruments necessary for the forms of cauterization; and I have arranged the chapters on the supremacy of the head among the parts.
In this procedure in this chapter by that method, so that the patient may praise God for it, because of what he will ask for.
And before I begin this, listen, son, and learn that in this chapter it is by means of fear what was in the first chapter about cautery.
And for this reason, it is necessary to be in great caution, because in the operation which is in this chapter many times evacuation of what is harmful to life depends on the puncture of the vein or incision over the abscess or opening of an exit or purification of the ulcer or extraction of a splinter or cutting of a stone and similar procedures. And with all of those things, accompany fear and trembling, and in the worst case of their severity, death.
And I warn you that this should not fall upon you for which there is blame, because some cases depend on your methods of treating people with many kinds of illnesses. And among them are some who become angry about their illness and are close to death because of the vehemence of what they find in their illness, and due to the length of their illness, and this illness brings fear which means death.
And among them is one who gives you all his possessions and says, "I hope for health from you," while his illness is deadly.
And therefore, you must not consent to one who comes to you with this attitude; and your caution should be greater than your greed and rapacity; and do not presume any reason about it unless based on certain knowledge that confirms what is before you, and which through experience proves worthy.
For the treatment of all your patients, make predictions and indications about which one's health can be restored, for in that is your help towards acquiring fame and reputation, praise and glory.
O, children, may God inspire your sound understanding and not take from you direction and protection, for these are in His hands.
And I have arranged this chapter distinctly by chapters as it follows the chapter on cauterization, from the treatment of the head down to the feet, so that what you seek and of which you have need may be clear to you.
First Chapter.
On the treatment of water that is contained in the head of children.
Know that this disease often affects children shortly after birth when the nurse presses too hard on the child's head.
And sometimes it happens for hidden causes that are not understood.
But I have seen this disease only in children; and in all those whom I treated, death followed, and thus it seemed to me that there was no remedy for them.
And I saw a child whose head was filled with water, and his head was so enlarged that he could not move because of the size of his head, and the moisture increased to such an extent that it caused death.
And this moisture either accumulates between the membranes and the bone, or it accumulates below the bone on the brain.
For when the operation is to be performed and the moisture lies between the bone and the abscess but is only slight, it is necessary that you cut through the middle of the head into a fissure in its length, and let this fissure run for two fingers' breadth where the moisture flows, and this is the shape of the spatum:
And if the moisture is more increased and the abscess is larger, then there are two such fissures made in this manner:
And if the moisture is beneath the bone, the sign of this is that you see the sutures of the head swollen on all sides and the fluid is pressed out when you press it with your hand at the surface but not deep inside and it does not return immediately. It is necessary that you cut through the middle of the head into three such fissures in this manner:
and then extract all the moisture; and then close the fissures with strips and bandages; and then apply ointment made of oil and wine on top from the fifth day, and then loosen the binding and treat the wound with tinctures and powders, and dress the head evenly; and eat only dry food with little moisture until the member is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, which you must consider according to the size of the abscess and the collection of fluid, for sometimes it is greater at the occiput, or in front, or on the right side, or on the left; and therefore pay attention to the incision where the abscess appears and the swelling of the fluid, and do this as best suits you.
And observe that you do not cut the artery, and beware of excessive bleeding and let the illness not persist due to that flow of blood and evacuation of moisture.
Chapter second.
When I treat certain wounds or ulcers in the hands or feet, and if it is old, and medication does not help in its cure, then the last resort is to begin with these two arteries.
And therefore, when you want to cut those arteries that the patient exposes by shaving their head, and then rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear, and then secure the patient's neck with a cloth; and then observe where the pulse is; and the locations of each near the places below the ears, and in some people they are hidden.
And then place your thumb on the artery, and then cut each one with a sharp scalpel, cutting deeply enough to reach the bone, and do this according to the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the scalpel below the artery and cut it where the incisions are made above.
And the length of the incision should be about two fingers wide, because when an artery is cut, blood spurts out in a pulsating manner, which comes out frequently.
But if the artery does not appear clearly, then measure from the ear a distance of three fingers, and then place your thumb there, and cut into the bone.
And the amount of blood to be let is about one uncie (ounce), and it should flow freely but not too much, and all this depends on the quantity that appears from the patient's strength and the fullness of the artery.
Now consider the wound, and if there remains any cause of swelling on the bone, then cleanse it with water so that an abscess does not form from cauterizing.
Now strengthen the wound with lint and cloths of linen, and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter III.
On the extraction of arteries that are in the temples.
When it happens that some acute illness or sharp fever occurs due to excessive moisture and heat in the veins of the temples, or if it is a chronic condition similar to these, and treatment with medicinal methods does not provide relief, then we resort to the method of extracting arteries in the temples, or cauterizing them as has been shown.
And the method of the operation and extraction of these is that the patient shaves the hair that is in the temples; and understand the artery that appears in the temple as it is shown to you by its pulse, and it remains hidden except in a few people due to the intensity of friction.
And if it is hidden from you, then tie the patient's head with some cloth; and then rub the area with a rough cloth or evaporate the area with hot water until the artery appears clearly. And now you take the spatula, which has this form: And then you carefully cut the skin with that tool with ease until you reach the artery; and then proceed to extract it in a manner that allows you to remove it from the muscle and reveal the arteries beneath layer by layer.
If the artery is fine, now wrap it at the joint, and then cut off a part according to the amount by which its extremities are lengthened and contracted, and there will be no flow of blood, for if they were not thus cut, the blood would not rise completely.
And then remove one ounce of blood within three days.
And if the artery is large, now make sure to tie it in two places with very strong thread or cords and do not allow it to putrefy before the consolidation of the wound, or else there will be a flow of blood.
And then cut away the excess that is between two ligatures; do this at that hour or another time.
And if you wish, cauterize that with a cautery near the bone or use a knife-shaped cautery after the extremities have been cut, and this will serve as a substitute for that procedure.
And it is better to perform this work if the patient has a fever or is of a heated complexion; for cautery helps eliminate residual moisture, and thus it is better in such cases.
And after the extraction of the arteries, it is necessary to fill the place with dried meat, and place it on a prepared powder, and then treat it with dry medicines that promote flesh growth until it is healed.
And if at another time during your work there is a flow of blood from the artery, now you must stop that blood, either with a cautery or by filling the place with dried meat, and press firmly until the blood is stopped. And if any of those causes is not present for you, then wait until the time has passed during which the blood must coagulate, and apply to the place with water of great coldness until the sharpness subsides, and bind it as seems fitting.
And what is quicker in the extraction of an artery is that you cauterize the vein with this cautery having two knives, after you have marked the two places with thread, and then place some ignited material until it reaches the bone and cuts the vein into two places, so that it stretches what is between its extremities, since they are not consolidated by this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And let the sharpness of both knives be examples. However, if you make it with less sharpness than two knives, it is better, for if it is as sharp as two knives, it approaches each roughness and does not cut the flesh quickly.
Therefore, when there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire is kept in them and the flesh is quickly severed.
And this work is better than all works and quicker.
And let the space between the two knives be the quantity of the thickness of a finger.
Chapter Four.
On the treatment of sharp and acidic tears from the eyes.
When there are acidic tears, and it is the condition of those veins which lie above the head outside, and you do not find any cause for the treatment of medicine in that, and often the face of the patient turns red and feels as if there were a crawling sensation on the forehead, and the eyes from this become weakened, producing so much moisture that even the hair around them is noticeable, and the eyelids are scorched by the sharpness of the tears. The ancient masters used to treat these conditions in such an operation, and did not name that treatment for delay.
And you command the barber to shave the hair on which are in the forehead area, and immediately incise three furrows equally distant according to the length of the forehead. And if the length of the incision is two digits: one incision should be equally distant at the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length a bit from the movement of the lids which are in the lacrimal area, and the third on the other side.
And guard against the beginning of the arteries which are on both sides, and extend your hand along the continuation of both jaws. And if the space between each incision is the quantity of three digits adjusted.
And if you have a piece of sponge prepared or dry cloth to press against the areas where you dry the blood.
And then insert the probe with two sharp points, which has this form for the incision as follows: which is from the first incision to the second incision. Excise along that entire ridge which is between two incisions on the bone, and then perform this incision midway between the first and second incision. And then spread this probe and insert into the first incision another instrument called a scalpel, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And pause the sharp side on the flesh adjacent to the ridge, and the blunt side within the bone, and proceed with it until you reach the middle incision, and dissect all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
May the incision be continued until the fluid appears at the root of the wound, and then place that fluid in the deeper part of the incision.
And when pus flows in equal quantity with the blood, express the places from which the clotted blood has been gathered.
And then apply pieces of fresh leather to each incision, and place powder mixed with vinegar, oil, and sour grape juice on them, and use plasters so that an abscess does not form by cauterization.
And on the third day, loosen the bandage and administer a generous infusion of hot water or decoction, and then treat it with basilicon ointment and rose oil until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
On the cure of tumors by catharsis and for descending humors from the internal parts of the head.
When there is a sudden and continuous need for catharsis in some cases, and the humors of the patient are depleted and small, and when paying attention to them shows debility, and the eyelids are ulcerated with hair loss, and at the temples there is acute pain and severe emaciation, then you should know from these symptoms that the matters and catharsis do not come except from veins originating in the internal parts.
And the best treatment for these cases is this: Command the patient to shave their forehead, and then make an incision in that area at its midpoint, a little over one finger's width (starting from the temple on the left side towards the right temple; and let the incision be through the bone; extend your hand beyond the temples which are hard due to mastication), until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth and then fill between the two lips of the fissure with hair or pieces of linen. And then sprinkle above the wound with powder mixed in oil so that it does not cause an abscess from cauterization.
And when you loosen this and see that the abscess has already diminished, then it is necessary to clean the bone starting from where the new flesh begins, and afterwards treat it with a drying regimen that will make the flesh grow, as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colocynth and mix them, and from these materials prepare a plaster which should be applied to these areas where the new flesh is growing.
Chapter VI.
On the treatment of what affects the ears.
Every cause that affects the ears is one of four types: it may be a mineral stone or a substance similar to stone, such as iron or lead; it may be from plants, such as thorns or beans; it may be fluid-like, such as water and sour wine and things similar to them; or it may come from an animal.
And so when a stone or any similar cause that does not adhere to the ear or mucous membrane enters the ear, place the ear towards the sun, and if you see the stone, then drip in some oil of violets or sesame into it, and afterwards administer its extraction with certain conditions, and make them sneeze by inducing a sneezing fit, and plug the nose before the onset of sneezing. And then place around the circumference of the ear some pieces of cloth or wool, and extend the ear upwards to the sides, as movement for several days is advisable with these causes.
And if it is not removed, then proceed with the extraction using the instrument called gesti epicicarios, which is subtle and of this form:
And if it is forcibly removed with the instrument, that is good; but if not, then proceed with the extraction using an even more subtle variation of the instrument after a few modifications.
And therefore, if it is not forcibly removed with that, then make a cannula of reed and insert the end of the cannula into the ear canal, and secure around the cannula with wax and softened oil so that nothing else enters except the cannula. And then extract with the small tool using strong traction, as described in the manner we have recounted.
But if it is not forcibly removed in this manner, then take a small piece of linen or a feather that birds are caught with, attach it to the end of a thread, and then wrap it carefully around the object. And then insert that into the ear canal with ease, and then dry the ear of its humidity.
And therefore, if it is not forcibly removed by all the means we have recounted, then you must consider cutting it before it becomes an abscess or spasm.
And the method of cutting it is that you begin by cauterizing the malady of the vein and extract the blood according to the manner appropriate for it, and then make a cut between your fingers and turn the ear upwards, and create a small incision at the edge of the ear in the place most pressed. And if the crack is of a crescent shape and reaches to the stone, and the stone found, remove that with appropriate instruments; and then promptly close the crack at that time with speed, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the cause lying in the ear is one of the gristle that grows and becomes inflamed, then administer the treatment and the extraction of it; but if you do not obey this instruction, at that time take a fine probe according to this form: and administer the removal of that gristle which is lodged in the ear.
And do not proceed with this when you think that it has already become moistened by the vapor of the ear, and pause at various times, and then extract it in small pieces or with delicate movements, or as we have said, for the extraction is facilitated.
But in the water that has entered the ear, it is good for the patient to use sneezing with cold at the beginning, and fill his own ear with some old cotton, and keep it over the ear where the water is, and if this expels it, well; but if not, then take small dry stones and lemons according to the length of a finger, and heat those in the fire for a little while, and let the patient insert one of them into the canal of his ear and jump on that side with a stone hitting the stone inside the ear. Then do not stop this with stones until all the water is expelled.
Then do not stop this with stones until all the water is expelled. And the water will be expelled in this way that you take paper or cotton, and put one end of it into the ear and extend the other end over fire until most of it is burned.
For this operation, insert another tube and pour water into it so many times that all the water comes out, and stretch that part with the tube as was said above about the stone extractor.
In the extraction of foreign bodies entering that ear, if it is a small object, proceed and treat it similarly as we have said in the division; but if it is a large object which causes pain to the patient, then manage the extraction by hand and incisions, and the cause of this is more serious than all other causes that fall into the ear.
In the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with the water as we have said in the division and in the treatise on distillations and your treatment does not provide relief, then it is necessary for you to expose the ear to the sun.
And therefore, if there appears any cause for the worms, then extract it by hand or with fine incisions; but if no cause appears, then take a tube that is narrow below and wide above, which has this shape: And insert the narrow end into the ear according to the quantity that will suffice for the patient's condition; then suck with a strong suction, and do this many times until all the worms are removed.
But if you do not adhere to this method, then plug and seal around the tube with wax as we have said; then administer distillations that have already been proven effective in treating worms, and you will find these described in the treatise on distillations.
And use the application with which you pour medicinal oils into the ear using this instrument, which has this shape: If you use a cup of silver or tin, make it tight, in which there is a small hole, and cover it above.
And thus it should be as they push in what is in the curvature of the cannula here where it will be necessary. And if you want, take the syringe and wrap its tip neatly with cloth, and then pour oil into the curvature of the ear, or juice from some herb, or any of these medicines in the cannula — and that is in the ear —, and then insert the syringe covered with cloth above and press it gently so that the oil is expelled into the curvature of the ear and the patient feels the oil inside.
And what is to be infused into the ear should be slightly warmed over fire. And be careful that the substance you will distill into the ear is not too strong, for medicines for the ear do not tolerate that.
Chapter VII.
On the treatment of ear blockages.
Sometimes infants are born with their mothers' wombs unruptured, and this also happens in some adults, where there is a blockage in the ear canal due to an infection or flesh growing there; and this obstruction sometimes occurs at the entrance of the ear canal, which cannot be seen, and sometimes it is in the parts outside.
And the one that occurs at the entrance of the ear canal, which cannot be seen, is among the most difficult conditions to treat.
And in that which you seek the vein, have the patient place his ear to the sun and observe it. And if you see swelling there, open it with a fine probe, which has the shape of this:
If the tip of it where there is a small width, of which one part is sharp and the other is not; and the probe has two sides so as not to hurt the ear.
And if the swelling is due to hidden flesh in the ear or in its canal, then use fine forceps and cut away the vein easily until all the flesh is excised.
And if the swelling is in front of the ear, then take a thin needle and heat it over fire for a moment, and then put it into the ear canal.
And if you have felt a swelling with what the patient perceives as ease, due to hearing difficulties, apply pressure to it gently; and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.

Chapter XII.

If the hair is too short, then attach a longer hair from the natural hairs to it, or leave it so that it grows out over some space, and then lift it.
Chapter XIII.

The method for the procedure is that you should cut open that contraction and separate its edges, then place between them a piece of linen cloth, and secure it there until they are healed.
You should not use treatments that dry out or constrict the ulcer, as using such methods will cause the ulcer to return worse than before and never heal properly.
