About the holes in the nostrils.
Most often is the hole in the nostrils narrow, and it is enlarged and treated over several days during which one must pay attention to it.
And for this reason, you should treat it at the beginning of its appearance, and you should continue to treat it entirely. And then pause on the place where you cauterized or applied a burning medicine, which was there instead.
But if the treatment is delayed until it needs to be enlarged, then consider whether it has become hardened, tough, dark in color, and with little sensation. At this point, do not touch it with a probe because often such an abscess becomes chronic; for most of the time, when you attempt to treat this abscess too late, it is due to great negligence towards it earlier.
And if the abscess is touchable and not dark in color, and if incision is possible at all, then evacuate the pus and incise it without fear, and treat the area with something that dries and constricts until it heals.
Chapter XXVI.
On suturing the nose and lips and the ear when the continuity is dissolved by plague or similar conditions.
Know that when dissolution of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, few perform this operation except on certain individuals.
Therefore, when there is any cause for those conditions, you should consider whether it is a fresh wound or with bleeding, and then you adjust the two sides of the wound with sutures, and afterward treat what lies within so that it may heal.
And if the edges of the continuity dissolution are already separated and each is made clean, then you should scrape away each part of its visible edge until blood appears. And then adjust the two edges with sutures and strengthen them, and over those apply powder of dragon’s blood and sprinkle it, and place on top a piece of diafinitive plaster or another consolidating plaster, and leave that area for two days or three, and then remove that and change the plaster and leave what lies within until the pus is completely absorbed, and then treat with an ointment so that it may heal.
But the method of suturing is that you join the dissolution of continuity either with needles as we have recommended for abdominal sutures, or join the edges of the wound with pus as appropriate to your judgment.
Chapter XXVII.
On the extraction of those causes that occur in the two edges in their internal parts.
Often in the internal parts of the edges of some people's wounds, there are hard abscesses similar to grease from goats, and some of them are smaller while others are larger.
Then convert the lips and fissures of each of those hardnesses from every side, and afterwards fill the place with fine cloth so that the blood may be restrained, and afterwards wash it well with something astringent and heal the place with water that is styptic until it is healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
On incising the flesh attached to the gums.
Great care must be taken when there is a growth on the flesh attached to the gum, which the ancients called "albulus." Then you should suspend that with a thread or hold it with forceps and incise it at its base, and allow the pus or blood to flow.
And afterwards apply fine cloth or other styptic powder or astringents to the wound.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it again, and cauterize it, for afterwards the cauterized area will not return.
Chapter XXIX.
On extracting teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth of the inner and outer parts and between the gums to which they are attached, there are rough spots, tumors, and some growths are blackened and become yellow or green until the gum becomes corrupt due to this corruption and the teeth become diseased.
And then you should make the patient sit between your hands and place his head in your lap, and rasp the teeth on which these rough spots appear until no such cause remains, and this will remove the blackness and yellowness and other such discolorations so that they are cleansed.
Thus if you are at that point in the reasoning from its beginning, it is good; and if not, then return to the reasoning on those points another day and on the second and third until it comes to the final statement in what you want.
And know that teeth have the need for various shapes and figures of forceps according to how they are prepared for your operation. And because the razors with which the tooth is cut are different from the razor used to file between the teeth according to other forms, and these forms of razors you should prepare those which you will see shortly:
Chapter XXX.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
Take care of the tooth from its pain with all gentleness and delay in total extraction, for there is no remedy that can replace it once it is extracted, since it is a substance sublime that deserves exemption from such removal.
But when the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, you must be certain which tooth is causing pain, for often the pain deceives the patient and they believe it to be in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one but immediately the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is removed, as we have already seen through the experience of surgeons.
Therefore, when you must be certain which tooth is painful, and once you know well, then it is necessary that you scrape around the tooth with a spatula having some virtue until it dissolves the tooth from the gum completely, and then move it using your fingers or with fine forceps little by little until it comes out.
Near the root of the tooth take strong pincers or forceps firmly, and place the head of the patient between your fingers so that he does not move, and then pull out the tooth straight in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause greater pain to the patient.
And then if it is not loose, take one of those instruments and place it below from all sides and try to remove the tooth in the same way as was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is fractured or decayed, then cover that part with cloth and bind it tightly to the root with a thin thread so that it does not break when you compress it with the forceps.
And it is necessary that you act extremely carefully when cutting around the entire gum area and be mindful of your strength so that you do not break it or leave any part behind, and return to the patient because it is better than the initial pain.
And be careful not to do what some ignorant barbers do in their rashness and audacity when removing teeth, as they do not administer what we have recommended; for often it leads to great harm to sick people which is much harder to remedy, and the reasons for this are entirely due to them or partially, or because they extract the tooth along with some bones of the jaw, as we have often testified.
And after the extraction of the tooth, wash the mouth with warm water or salt water.
And if there is bleeding due to this, as often happens, then take some quantity of egg yolk and apply it to the wound, and if that does not stop the bleeding, cauterize it.
The shape of the forceps and thin tongs with which the tooth is grasped at the beginning should be long in length, short in handle, and thick, so that they are not bent when you work with them.
And they should be like bars of thick handle so that when you turn them the tongs are not bent, they should have short extremities.
And they should be made of Indian iron for the straight parts and at the ends should have teeth that fit into one another and turn by strong constriction, and sometimes the ends should be in the shape of a bird’s beak called a ciconia, which is a kite, and they are of strong constriction.

Chapter XXXII.
On the treatment of teeth that protrude beyond the others.
When the prominent teeth do not follow their natural course due to their form being lax, especially when this occurs in women and more so in those who are venerable; therefore, you should consider: if a tooth is very close to another tooth, and there is no possibility of extraction or treatment without causing damage, then root out that one. But ensure it does not attach to the adjacent tooth; at this point, you incise it with an instrument of this shape; it resembles a pig's tusk, and it is made of Indian steel with an extremely sharp tip.
And let your incision be such that when you incise it over several days due to the hardness of the tooth, it does not become inflamed or affect other teeth.
And if it is a prominent tooth that needs filing, then file it with an Indian steel file of this shape:
And let it be entirely made of Indian steel and grind it appropriately; it should have very fine points as with a file used to make needles, with which the tooth is filed little by little over several days with ease, so that other teeth are not inflamed or harmed. Along the edge lines there, and cut that with razors.
And if it is a tooth of which one part is broken and you shape the tongue, immediately file that inside where the sharpness of that fracture is smoothed out, and fill it so that the tongue is not injured and the speech is not impaired.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the restoration of defective teeth with gold or silver filling.
When it happens that the front teeth are loosened or dislocated by impact or accident, and the patient cannot bite properly due to this, and you have treated them with astringent medicines, but your treatment has not been effective, then because they do not bite correctly, attach those that are strong with gold and not silver, for silver changes color and soon deteriorates, whereas gold never changes.
And let the filling be of such subtlety and thickness according to the quantity that can enter between the teeth; and the form of the work is that you take the filling and place twice as much of it between two healthy teeth, and then build up with two tortuosity of the filling between one or more defective teeth, until it reaches the surface of the healthy tooth on the other side. And then continue the filling from the part where you started, and strengthen your work with ease and skill so that it is not defective.
And when you strengthen the filling, be careful not to injure the roots of the teeth.
And then trim the excess filling with forceps or scissors, and shape those parts as needed, and place them between the healthy tooth and the defective one so that they do not injure the tongue. And thus he leaves it as narrow as a Roman would.
If it is loose or ruptured, strengthen it or those parts with another thread in such a way for all its duration.
And this is the form of teeth and the figure of the alignment of two healthy teeth and of two defective teeth, as you sometimes see them joined:
And when it happens that one tooth or two are returned to their proper place, and they grow back as we have recounted, and remain in place.
And only a good artisan and wise person can do this.
And sometimes a cause for using cow bones is given, and it is shaped like a tooth, and placed in the location where the tooth is missing, and it is strengthened as we have said, and remains and serves as that tooth for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the incision of the membrane that is in the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament that is below the tongue sometimes is natural and one has it from birth, or it is accidental and occurs due to an injury that has already healed.
The operation is to open the mouth of the patient, with their head tilted back; and lift their tongue, and then cut that ligament along the nerve according to its width, ensuring it is freed from the root.
But if there is any hardness or knot in it, and this has occurred due to the healing of an injury, then you should incise it carefully and cut it along its width until the knot is dissolved.
And be careful that no cut is made in the skin and that you do not sever here any artery or vein, causing a flow of blood.
And then let the remnants of the incision be washed with cold water or vinegar or salted water; and afterwards place a licorice stick there; this is how the bad taste that accumulates under the tongue should be removed each night so that it does not consolidate elsewhere.
But if blood flow occurs, place over the area triturated vitriol; but if the blood spurts, then immediately cauterize the spot with a hot iron, which is appropriate for such cases. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter XXXV.
On the Extraction of a Ranula Under the Tongue.
In some cases, an abscess similar to a small swelling occurs under the tongue, preventing it from performing its natural function; and quickly it enlarges to the point of filling the mouth.
And the procedure is that you should open the mouth wide in front of the light and examine the abscess.
And if you see it as a dark or black color, hard, and one that the patient does not feel any sensation in, then do not touch what is clearly gangrenous.
And if it appears inclined towards a whiter color, which is moist, then you should place your finger there, and cut it with a sharp scalpel, cutting it completely; and if blood spurts during the operation, immediately apply crushed zinc sulfate, also known as vitriol, to control the bleeding.
Then return to your work after it has been treated thoroughly with water and salt; and care for it with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter xxxvi.
On the incision of an abscess in both armpits and that which is near the groin, and of other abscesses.
When you find an abscess in the groin area, it involves glands that are clustered together and appear outside the body, known as armpits. If you treat them as we have described in the division and they do not heal, then consider whether the abscess is hard, dark in color, and insensitive. At this point, do not touch it with an instrument.
And if it is red in color and the root is large, then do not touch it with an instrument due to the flow of blood. Instead, leave it until it has matured on its own, and either you will then extract it, or it will burst open by itself.
And if it is white in color, soft, and the root of it is delicate, then that should be incised.
And the procedure is that you consider, before your operation, whether the abscess has already rested and healed somewhat on its own or if it has diminished in any way. Then make the patient sit facing the sunlight, and place his head to one side, and expose the mouth of the abscess. The assistant should hold firmly the area around the opening with their hand, and then use a noble instrument of this form:
And it should be made of silver or iron, and as thin as a knife.
Thus, when you press with your finger upon that tongue and the abscess is shown to you, then apply heat over it firmly; now take some honey and place it in an opening, and draw it out towards the outer parts as much as you can. Another one you pull out from the cause of the tunics, and then cut it with an instrument whose form is as follows: and it is similar to forceps, but the ends of it are curved, so that the opening of each one of them is aligned with the other, with sharp points, which should be made of iron.
But if this instrument is not available to you, then cut it with a scalpel whose form is as follows: It should be sharp on one side and blunt on the other side.
And after you have cut one incision, cut the other in the same manner, and then wash the wounds with clean water.
But if there is bleeding, wash with water boiled with lemon peels or myrtle leaves and similar astringents until the flow of blood is stopped, and then treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes there appear in the throat other abscesses similar to those two ears, and cut them as I have said regarding the incision of two incisions similarly.
And I had treated a woman with an abscess that was born in her throat, which abscess tended to have a dark color and little feeling, it had already closed the throat, and she breathed through a narrow passage, and she had been forbidden from eating and drinking water, and she would have reached death if it had not been for my intervention within one or two days.
The abscess had burst so much that it discharged through two channels within the two rims.
Then I inserted it fistularly and placed it in one of their incisions, and pulled that out, and what was removed was of the large portion, and then I proceeded to that which included from the other side, and then I closed its mouth, and comprehended its tongue, and then I placed the incision in that abscess.
Then I removed a large part of it, and only a little blood ran from it. Then the girl was completely recovered, and she began to drink water right away, and soon after she started to eat food. Therefore, I did not stop incising that abscess for many times over a long period, and an abscess succeeded in that place, which I incised until it was prolonged.
Then I applied something and cauterized the girl's wound, then it became adhered; and afterwards I walked around and did not know what God would do with that right after me.
Chapter xxxvii.
On the incision of the cheek when it is abscessed.
When mucus is present in the cheek and it is abscessed and elongated, it is then called perpendicular; and if it is in the lower part thick and round, it is then called a bubo.
However, when it is cured as we have described in the division and does not conform to your treatment, and you see that it has already abscessed and is subtle, then it is necessary for you to incise those that are aggregated and round; and if it is of such length and is bloody, dark or black, and is not at the center, then it is necessary for you to attempt its incision, because there is fear of malady in that.
And when you see that it is according to the method I have said about its whiteness and length, that you make the patient sit and press the tongue with an instrument of which the form suits it, and place the incision in the new position, and pull it down and incise it with one of two instruments which are mentioned in the incision of two membranes.
And you should not incise it beyond the natural limit, for if you incise too much, you will impair their speech.
And after the incision, use what we have recommended for the incision of two membranes, and care for it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision, you should use an anesthetic during cauterization to alleviate their fear. The method of the cautery is nothing but with sharp water. Place the head of the patient in your lap, and then press the tongue with the instrument we have described. Then take the water we mentioned in the chapter on cauterization and apply it in a warm manner, and pour it not thick nor thin into the concavity of the instrument whose shape is this:
Let the extremity of the concavity in which the medicine is placed be like the concavity of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the palate, and the patient lies on their back so that saliva or mucus from the mouth does not flow out due to the internal medicine, thus preventing any loss of it.
And then with your hand on the medicine, apply it to the uvula for a short while until you see it become blackened and numbed by the bite of the medicine.
And if you wish, take a sponge and wrap it around the tip of the reed, and immerse the sponge in the medicine, and insert the reed with the sponge into a tube so that the sponge adheres to that tube, and do this as many times as needed until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the grape, and then leave it alone until it dries and falls off after three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, do so, and then after the cauterization clean around the grape with a sponge dipped in vinegar, and modify what is around it from the medicine, and wash it with salt water, and treat it externally with embrocation, and internally with remedies until it is healed.
And this kind of treatment is safer than incision and more enduring in terms of fear.
In some cases, the grape is cured with something that cauterizes and incises less severely, as it is treated by subfumigation in this manner: take wax, hyssop, thyme, rue, Arabian saffron, chamomile, and similar herbs, and mix all of these or some of them in a container, and immerse them in sour wine, which should be boiled; the pot should be moderately heated, and at the bottom of the pot place a tray, on top of which place an instrument made of reed according to this method; it can be made of silver or reed.
And insert the end into the mouth of the patient so that the vapor reaches the uvula through the tube, until the uvula is sufficiently treated.
Regarding the incisions made within what is dry tissue, be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the onset of abscesses, for it would be too early for the abscess, and you should not do that, but rather wait for the development of the abscess clearly.
If this instrument is not available to you, now take a cane and attach at its end a cover of cloth, so that the mouth of the wound is covered, for the covering of cloth prevents the vapor from entering the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter XXXVIII.
On the extraction of thorns and what adheres to the skin and other causes.
Incisions in the tissue adhering to the skin or a fish spine or some other cause.

And what does not appear and is hidden in the throat, you should make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for the cause attached to the throat will be easily expelled by vomiting; or have the patient swallow a piece of bread or lettuce juice or a bite of dry bread; or take a piece of dried sea sponge and tie it firmly with thread, and then have the patient swallow that, as when it reaches the location where thorns are lodged, they will be easily expelled. Take one eel and boil it in vinegar, then remove its intestines and extract the contents; or take a poultice and apply the preparation to it and let it be treated with the extract.

Chapter XXXIX.
On the extraction of a leech attached to the throat.
When a leech is treated with what we have said in the division of treatment with medicines and not compresses, now consider the patient's throat in sunlight, since you have retracted the tongue of the patient with the instrument that we have recommended.
Then if you see the leech, remove it gently with a small forceps or with fine tweezers appropriately.
And if it is not possible for you to remove it with that, now take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and then place a heated iron in the concavity of the cannula, and do this many times (until the leech dies and falls off) and have the patient abstain from water for the entire day.
Take a piece of flax soaked in water and place it on the wound, and work it into the mouth of the cavity, and wash out the mouth with that; and do not let any cause make you remove from there anything else and change the water every hour with its own hand, for as soon as the leech feels the water it will bite.
But if it is not possible to perform what we have recommended with this, then fumigate the wound with cimex or asafetida by means of the instrument which we have described for the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it may fall out.
And there is another method of operation in fumigation of this, which is that you take an urn in which are burning coals and the urn is covered with a cover in the middle of which is a hole, and attach the end of the instrument to that hole; and then proceed above for the fumigation; and place the wound over the mouth of the tube, and close it so that the fumigation does not escape until you know that the fumigation has reached the leech, for the leech will immediately fall out.
And if it does not bite, then proceed with the fumigation as many times as necessary, and prevent the patient from drinking, and give causes that are salty and dry, and let them not drink water, and in this way it is necessary for this regimen to be followed.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is removed from the body when it is seen; and it resembles forceps as you see, but there is some curvature to it: which is placed in the joint, and its extremity resembles the beak of a stork, which is a kind of claw, on which there are grooves, so that when it presses upon any cause, it does not slip.
Chapter XL.
In which is the summary of the discourse on the preparation for abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses are various types of swellings, in many ways, as will be recalled for each one in this book.
And they differ in their puncture and incision by two methods, of which one is according to the nature of the abscess itself; and by what presses upon the humidity.
And the second method is according to the locations where they occur in the body, for an abscess occurring in the head is different from one occurring in the buttocks; and an abscess occurring in a fleshy part is different from one occurring in a joint; and each has its treatment by surgical operation.
And there are some abscesses that should not be punctured but left until the pus has properly matured or they have reached completion.
There are other abscesses that should be treated when they are crosswise and not properly matured, such as those near joints, for when an abscess is near a joint, its nature extends around it until you can reach what is inside it, and if the ligaments or nerves of that joint become corrupted because of it, it becomes a cause of contraction of that member; or if it is near a principal limb, and if you delay puncturing it before it has matured, it may harm the principal limb; or if it is near the buttocks, then puncture it when it is not fully matured; but do not puncture it raw, without proper digestion, except what is near the buttocks, so that you do not leave behind pus which can extend to deeper parts of the buttocks and cause a fistula, or that it be done in such a way that it cannot heal. May it be known that you should know the time for piercing the abscess, and this is when it is ripe.
And you should know that it is ripe when there is a reduction in pain and the cessation of fever, and soon after a decrease in redness and pulsation, and the sharpness at the head of the abscess, and other signs.
And you should know that the piercing should be done in the place where the pus is below the abscess and comes from the highest part of the swelling.
And the piercing should always be done along the length of the flesh or body if the abscesses are in parts like the limbs or joints, and in places away from veins, tendons, nerves, arteries; and finally, in all places equal to those where there is no reflection; for in those that do reflect, use piercing according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy place, the best time is to delay the piercing until the second stage of digestion is confirmed, as we have said; for if you pierce it before that, it prolongs the suffering of the patient and increases corruption, and makes the lips of the abscess hardened and its depth greater.
But some abscesses should be pierced according to the width of the flesh or body due to necessity, or according to what the limb requires.
And you should administer a small piercing, and in large abscesses, a larger and wider piercing or multiple sections according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And other abscesses in which the treatment should be hollowed out and cut open, when it is done as if with bread dough and is performed at a time when it is ready, just as occurs with those that happen from excessive debility and buboes.
And of those which are incised with a section having three angles; and of those is in which it is incised as the figure of a myrtle leaf, as the abscesses of the fingers.
And of those in which round section and lunar section are administered, similar to these.
But in the case where there is none such as their superficial lion abscesses, it is necessary that you pierce it with a superficial puncture, or simply scrape it.
And when the abscess is large and has already collected pus and unhealthy matter, you should pierce it but do not hesitate to extract all the pus at that time; having extracted one part from it, on another day scrape the abscess inside, and then extract a larger part, and continue doing this several times according to its degree of inflammation.
And especially if the patient is a young man, or pregnant woman, or small child, or elderly in debility, for the animal spirit's many visits are resolved with the evacuation of the pus, and the patient easily recovers, and you will heal them.
Therefore, guard this chapter with great caution, and after your puncture in which you pierce these abscesses, it is necessary that you cleanse the wounds and then consider whether the wound of the abscess is small or if it is a flat section; at that time administer some of them with lint or old cloth. And if the abscess is large and the cracks of the puncture are many, then it is necessary that you place wax in each groove until they heal together.
And if in the abscess a part of the cord has been cut or has become concave around it, then you should fill it with old cloth or thread made from linen, as when you treat ulcers or roots without moisture; and strengthen that on the third day; and afterwards proceed with it and care for it according to what is needed of ointments until it is healed.
If blood flows during the time of your operation, then at that hour administer water mixed with vinegar in which you dip a cloth, and place that over the site of the bleeding for several times.
If the bleeding persists, then you should administer treatment and cure as well as powders which I have recommended in various places of this our book and division.
If it is in the time of winter, and the site of the abscess is a place with many nerves, then you should administer powders mixed with vinegar and hot oil, and apply those to the sites.
And if it is in summer, and the sites are fleshy, then you should apply powders soaked in water and oil or in vinegar and oil, and keep this moistened until the third day, as we have said, and then you should open the abscess and clean it and administer to its cure what it needs until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the cure of abscesses generally, but according to the method of distinction I have already said about each abscess in what manner is the way of its cure separately by chapters.
Chapter 41.
On the section of abscesses which appear in the neck.
In the neck of the head, small abscesses appear, and they are of various types of knots that contain membranes; and their types vary.
And they are soft, and those contain moisture similar to jelly. Of those things which contain humidity like dust, and similar to them; and of those things which are stony, hard.
And in the section of all, and in the extraction of those there is no fear, as you should know that the extraction of those does not contradict the art of surgery.
And the operation in the section of those is that you express them with an instrument whose form approaches this, which is called an entremes, until you know what it contains.
For if what it contains is humidity, then you should cut it with a section according to its length by making a superficial section according to this form:
And begin the section from the line of .B. to the line of .G. Then, as the humidity is evacuated, you should remove the skin that contains this moisture, and do not leave any part of it behind, for if any part remains, it may lead to further issues returning.
And then submerge a piece of lint in Egyptian ointment if it is available, and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with this and leave it for another day, for this will dissolve everything that remains of that membrane.
And then apply the lint again with Egyptian ointment a second and third time if necessary, until you are confident that there is no remaining part of that membrane; now treat the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if an abscess contains hard nodules, then you should incise it according to this form: This is a cross-shaped incision, and place the point of your instrument between two lips of the incision and scrape it all around, and exert your strength in extracting the membrane which contains it.
If your surgery encounters difficulties, now do what I have first recounted to you.
And similarly proceed with the incision and treatment of the abscess as we have told you; and an incision on a calcified abscess is easier, because it contains little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes there are cases of abscesses in the head in some people who do not have much moisture throughout; the reason for this is that I once incised an abscess on an old woman's head and found it as hard as a stone, rough, white, and could not be extracted, and if anyone were to strike it with force, it would injure them.
However, in other abscesses that occur in the head, this is not the case of this chapter; likewise, for abscesses that appear on infants' heads and behind their ears, the incision should be superficial.

Chapter XLII.
On the incision of scrofulas which occur in the neck.

Chapter XLIX.
On the Incision of Abscesses That Occur Due to Artery or Vein.
When an artery is ruptured and the membrane covering it has become consolidated, then abscesses may occur. The signs by which it can be known that inflammation and abscess are due to the artery or vein: for when the abscess is due to an artery, it tends to form along the front of the body; and if you press on the abscess with your fingers, you will hear a rustling sound in the area.
And that which is from a vein is round at the surface of the body.
And sectioning this abscess is an error, especially in those areas where the armpits are, and in the angles, and on the neck, and in many places on the body; for it is dangerous, therefore it should be avoided that its treatment may involve iron, and those that are in the extremities or on the head should also be avoided.
In the case where it is due to inflammation of the artery opening, make an incision in the longitudinal section; and near the place where the incision should be made with hooks near the artery, and separate the tunics until it is exposed.
And then place the needle there and make it penetrate into the other side, and tie the artery with a double thread at two places, which I explain in detail when extracting two arteries that are in the thighs.
And then press the area between two bandages with a cloth so that the blood stops flowing entirely while it is there, and the abscess dissolves.
And then administer treatment in which poultices are applied so that the bandages can be removed; and then treat it with appropriate ointments so that it may heal.
And if it is an abscess from the section of a vein, now you should have in your hand what is possible regarding the abscess along its longitudinal direction, and then insert the needle below the place where you had your hand, and make it penetrate; and tie a thread around it double so that you can extract it from the other side, and then ligate the abscess with a strong bandage as I have told you in the grapevine bandaging of both sides along the two extremities of the thread.
If you fear that you will remove the thread, then at that hour insert the needle with the wax under all the abscess. You should make the insertion of the needle first, and strengthen its place in four places.
When the abscess is dry in its middle part and when what is inside it has come out, then you should incise the excess of the cord, and leave what is attached to it.
And then sprinkle on the powder which you have mixed with wine and oil, and afterwards administer the treatment made with cords and herbs until it is healed.
Chapter on the abscess that occurs due to twisting of the nerves.
Just as an abscess can occur in arteries and veins, similarly, an abscess occurs in the nerves when they are struck or affected by excessive labor, and for similar reasons.
And it is done with the needle in the areas of the joints or the heel, or in any place where the joints are painful.
And it is a severe abscess, and its color resembles that of the surrounding tissue, and it is among the worst conditions when it is tight due to tension, and when the patient appears in a state similar to stupor.
And it is not an abscess that aggregates in the front of the body, and for this reason it is below the cord, and it pains throughout without going to the parts before or behind.
Therefore, what is in the joints should not be wrapped in iron, for it will quickly lead to contraction.
Make a cut in the head or forehead with a spatum (a surgical instrument). If it is a small abscess, then cut it out with forceps and remove it by root.
And if it is large, then suspend it with an incision, and drain it; then apply dry dressings; and close the wound with sutures, and care for it until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
On the incision of an abscess that occurs on the lip as it would occur on the abdomen.

And I have seen one case where there were two parts on his abdomen that resembled mushrooms, and there was no difference between them, they were white and had fine roots.
And the edges of those were inverted and split open, and moisture constantly flowed from them.
Therefore, I incised them and found inside one weighing about eighteen uncias and the other about ten uncias.
And the procedure in incising them is that you should consider whether the malady is moist, and if the color of the abscess is white and moist with a fine root; then you incise it with a broad spatum, and immediately in your presence cauterize it over the fire, because in such cases the section should expel much blood; and if bleeding occurs, cauterize that area.
And if the wound is small and frightening, guard against making an incision with a knife; instead, take fresh lead and apply it to that place in the manner we have described; and leave it for two days.
And then adjust it in the tightness of the lead thread; therefore, do not stop applying more lead as needed until it is firmly set and falls by itself when its work is done.
But if the wound is of thick tissue, there is some cause for fear and dread at its incision, especially in those that are in the abdomen.

And then take care of it until the wound is healed.

Chapter LII.
On the Treatment of Hernia of the Navel.
Hernia of the navel is caused by various reasons; it may be due to fissures in the abdominal wall above the navel, and through this, either a membrane or bowel protrudes as in other ruptures; or it can be from blood that flows from veins or arteries depending on the case, or from trapped air in that area.
If it is through a crack in the scaphoid and pus issues from the tissue, then the color of the abscess is similar to the color of the skin; it causes severe pain, and soon appears in various situations. And if it is through the protrusion of the bowel, then its situation differs greatly from what we have recounted about other abscesses; when you press that area with your fingers, it feels concealed, and soon returns; and very lightly it is felt there, and it is magnified by means of warm baths and vigorous work.
If it is due to moisture, then it is soft and not concealed when pressed by your hand, nor is it constricted, nor is it hard.
And if it is due to blood, then the abscess appears with a declining black color.
If it is due to necrotic flesh, then the abscess is severe, large and hard, and it penetrates according to a certain quantity.
If it is due to gas, then the touch of it is soft.
And the procedure in such cases is that you must consider whether the swelling of the navel is due to arterial blood, or venous blood, or gas; then be cautious about treating it, for there is fear and dread as I have taught you in the chapter where I discussed abscesses due to arteries and veins.
And if the swelling of the navel is due to the bowel or tissue, then you must instruct the patient to take their clothing off and stand extended; then examine around the entire navel with your hand, and then instruct them to lie down on their back; then surround the area marked by your examination around the navel with a bandage made of linen strips or cloth in an appropriate manner; then extend half of the abscess above about one inch; then bind it around the circumference firmly or loosely as is fitting.
From the side of the abscess let the membrane be extended over the bandage and insert your finger into that pus and search for the sack; and if you have recognized or guarded it by means of the bandage, now immediately extract it and urge on the sack to the parts inside the belly.
And if you have found the tissue, now take that one ounce; and cut into the excesses of it.
But if it is contrary to your artery or vein, now encircle that spot, and then return to your operation; and take two needles and place them in those two strong threads and insert the two needles into the circuit you have made around the abscess in a cross shape, which you should make penetrate; and then divide the abscess into four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the place open so that the extended flesh can drain and fall off by itself; or incise it when it has thinned out and will drain, and then treat that area with water as needed until it is healed.
And if the prominence of the navel is of flesh or due to moisture, now it is necessary that you conquer the abscess as we have said and extract the blood or moisture which you find there, and then treat that area with what is needed and consolidate the place.

On the Treatment of Ulcers.

But if it is contrary to you to cut thick blood from a vein or artery, then cauterize that until the flow of blood ceases, and then treat it with other methods until it is healed.
Chapter 56.
On the Treatment of Hydrophobia.
We have already recommended, in the division, on the types of hydrophobia from water, and the signs of each type, and the treatment of it with medicines.

And when you have already treated this type of hydroptic tympanites with medicines and do not confirm your treatment, now consider whether the sick person has become weakened or is suffering from another sickness such as hydroptic, just as if it were coughs or other chest conditions similar to these. Now guard yourself that you do not treat them with iron, for there is fear regarding this.
But if you see the sick person is robust in health, and in him there is no other sickness but hydroptic alone, and neither child nor old person, then the method of the operation on him is that you lift the patient between your hands, and have the minister approach and squeeze his belly with his hand, expelling the water from below the navel, and afterward take a sharp probe in this manner:
it should be of two sharp points long, with a sharp end like a probe; but it should have some slight curvature little by little, so that in the operation you do not pass into the bowel and thereby injure him.

But if the origin of the hydroptic condition is from disease of the kidneys, then your incision should be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if the generation of it is from the gall, then your incision must be on the right side.
And it is not necessary for your incision to be on the side where the patient wants to lie down because this would cause the superfluities to rush to that place, which would be harmful.
And then perforate with the instrument all of the gallbladder, and then insert into that section, and raise your hand between the gallbladder and the duct, and you will extract it; and if the quantity is that of one fingernail or so.
And then perforate the duct so that it reaches an empty space, which is where the fluid will drain. And then extract that and insert an instrument which has the following form: and it is similar to a thin silver cannula; it is made of silver or ivory or lion leather, at the lower part of which there is a small hole, and on the sides there are three holes: two from one side and one from the other side; and the tip of it is cut like a feather, as you can see.
And at the upper end of it is a ring, because when the instrument reaches the fluid, you should immediately stop using it so that only a moderate amount of fluid is evacuated, for if you evacuate too much in one go, the patient may die from resolution of their vital spirit, or it could lead to imminent death.
But evacuate it according to the strength of the patient and according to how their condition, the quality of their pulse, and their overall state indicate to you.
Here is the translation:

Chapter LVI.
On the swelling that occurs in the foreskin at the head of the penis, and on its corruption, blackness, and adherence of the foreskin to the head of the penis.
Such occurrences often happen with this swelling in the penis and it is a chronic flesh prominence.
And one of these is malignant and the other is non-malignant.

Chapter LXIX.
On Castration.
Know that castration in our law is excommunicated, but for the reason it was read to me, I remember it in this book; and the remembrance of it is not necessary except by two ways, one of which is so that when asked about it, the physician knows how to treat those who are affected by it; and the other way is that we often have need of castration for certain animals for our benefit from them, such as horses, oxen, pigs, and similar beasts.
And therefore I say that castration is performed in two ways: either by atrophy or by cutting and section.
But in the case where it is done by atrophy, the method of the operation is that we should immerse the animal in warm water until the testicles become softened and are suspended. And then take those with your hand until they become soft and do not appear to be hard upon touch.

Chapter 70.

In women, there is a special case: the female organ above the pubis appears as two testicles of a man slightly raised towards the inside, like the scrotum of a man and two testicles.

And in the second species of men who have urine that is in the cord of the testicles, there is no cure or operation for it.

Of the treatment of fistulas that arise from hemorrhoids.
Fistulas that arise in the area of hemorrhoids with pus, are hard and thick, which arise near the anus or in the space between one part.
And it is either one fistula or multiple.
And as that hardness becomes old and runs with watery white moisture or subtle pus, and sometimes these fistulas are superficial, while others penetrate.
And those that penetrate are known by what is emitted from them through discharge and swelling; and sometimes the nature of their discharge reveals their true condition, and it is easily discerned.
And sometimes these fistulas extend into the space penetrating towards the bladder, and into the urethra; and some of those that penetrate reach the joint of the spine and into the bone marrow.
And to distinguish a penetrating fistula from one that does not penetrate, you should insert your finger into the anus, and gently introduce a thin probe of iron or wood into the fistula when there is no turbulence.
But if there is turbulence, then gently insert a thin probe of lead or horsehair into the fistula until you feel the probe or hair with your finger.
And if you do not feel anything with your finger and there is no discharge or swelling or ventilation as we have said, then you know that it is non-penetrating, and now proceed to the treatment.
When the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the shaft of the penis, then its significance is such that urine comes from there and it prohibits doing anything to it until the place in medicine becomes consolidated.
If it penetrates into the joint of the thigh or into the bone and causes pain in the area, then the sign is that pus can be felt here if there is not a twist within the bone, and pain in the circuit of the affected part; and that it will be purulent at all times; and that this treatment does not help in such cases, nor is consolidation possible without incision.
And in all those penetrating fistulas there is no certain cure, and the cure for those is the labor and vanity of many physicians, upon which they rely firmly.

On the treatment of plagues.
I have already stated in the division of the treatment of plagues what was suitable for the book, and I will now describe the treatment of those with medicine according to the completion.
Therefore, I say that plagues are diverse according to how the plagues are caused and according to the location where the plagues occur.
And the causes for which plagues often occur are such as stone formation, or incision by a sword or knife, or striking with a spear, or with a stick, or with an arrow, or similar to those from various causes.
Here are the plagues that are in the parts of the body such as the plague which attacks the head, the neck, the feet, the belly, the groin, and similar ones in the limbs.
And I recommend the treatment of certain plagues, so that you pause that reasoning and rule over other plagues.
And I will begin with the simple plagues of the head, for the memory of the compound ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say that when you encounter a simple plague in the head and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is due to a stone or something similar and only the skin has been broken, making it a large swelling and you fear there may be an abscess underneath, at this moment you should take action and perform phlebotomy on the cephalic vein immediately, without delay; and extract as much blood from that area according to the quantity of the strength required unless some reason prevents phlebotomy, especially if the blood coming out of the wound is minimal.
And let the phlebotomy be from the opposite side of the affected area; and keep the patient from overeating and from heavy foods.
But if some reason prohibits phlebotomy, then reduce the patient's food intake and do not allow meat or rich foods.
And pause concerning the plague, if an abscess forms underneath it; then apply rose oil alone or in a substance that has been stypticized.
But if you are sure of an abscess underneath, then pause concerning the plague, especially if it is fresh with its own blood and the air has not changed the powder that I recommend to apply in small amounts.
And if the weather has already changed that, then pause for some time until it is healed.
And if the wound is large or due to a wound with similar characteristics and the lips of the wound cannot be approximated by simple means, then approximate those using sutures as I will describe regarding the suturing of such wounds.
And if the wound is already exposed to the bone and deeper structures and there is no need for retention but only a small suspensorium, then incise it and treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh in place of the granulation tissue, and instruct the patient to eat foods that are nutritious: meat, fish, and similar foods until he is well-composed.
And if you find putrefaction in the granulation tissue and it does not adhere to the bone, then incise all of that necrotic tissue and remove it, and subsequently treat it.
And if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood flow can occur and it does not coagulate with medicines, then inquire about the artery, and if you find that unincised, then incise that with a scalpel, or ligate that; and if necessity provokes that what we have said does not suffice, then cauterize until the bleeding is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then the cause of them is minor; therefore, you can powder those with this powder, as it will consolidate them before the weather changes.
And the method for the powder is as follows: take oil of sesame one part, blood of dragon two parts, and burnt copper three parts; mix all thoroughly and sieve it, and fill the wound with it, and secure it with good compression so that it adheres well to the wound, and let it coagulate with the blood.
From above the wound apply plasters and let that remain there, and do not remove it while it is still such that it has not become inflamed, for medicine does not reach it until it is healed.
And sometimes this cure is used alone when it is not at hand the juice and blood of the dragon.
And now this method of operation is used for large wounds when it is necessary to place upon them and narrow them, and on fresh wounds with their blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, especially when it is in summer, then there is no intention to apply this powder, but at that time cure those with what we have mentioned, namely placing upon them some cold applications or placing upon them flour of barley with water and honey until they begin to decay; and then cure those with the other treatment until they are healed.
But if it happens with the wound that there is a fracture in the bone, and it is small, then you extract it with a probe.

Of the wounds of the abdomen.
The injury that occurs in the abdomen is either severe, slight, or moderate.
And a small disruption is difficult to handle by other means, and it should be noted that the reduction of them is more difficult if you do not carefully return them to their place immediately, as they tend to harden and swell, making the instrument for their manipulation difficult.
And therefore, the best injury is moderate, for in that case it is not difficult to reduce the bowel, whereas it is difficult in both of the previous types mentioned.
And you should know that the suturing of the abdomen is done in four ways, but two types of stitching are common and suitable for sutures of other wounds on the body.
Of two other sutures proper communicate with other wounds, but however more in the suture of the abdomen.
And I recommend to you each exposition and declaration.
Therefore I say that when the wound is small and it is caused by some reason in the intestine, and it becomes difficult to reduce that, then there are two ways: either because of the smallness of the rupture as we have said, or when the intestine has become inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore when that is so, then it is necessary for you to warm that in such a way that you submerge a sponge or cloth in lukewarm water alone, or soak it in hot water with cyperus and spica, and apply it to the intestine until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes I use that which has styptic properties, and it is better than water alone for resolving the inflammation.
And when you see the inflammation resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which honey and mallows have been steeped, for by this the instrument of pain is alleviated slightly.
But if the instrument of pain persists near this cure, then you should divide the rupture slightly with an instrument on which hemorrhoids have been cut.

Of the instrument, this is its form:
One part of the sharp end of the probe, and the other part not sharp, and the tip subtle, and not as fine as a needle point, but somewhat blunt.
And thus when the wound is enlarged and the edges are separated from the fascia, now it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form in its proper place, if possible, for this is better.
But when the disruption is wide and it is in the lower part of the abdomen, now you must make the patient lie on his back, and place his arms closer to his head than usual; and if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then you place his head and feet higher than the rest.
And similarly, if the disruption is in one of the two sides of the abdomen, now you must constantly direct your attention and consideration so that the part where the wound is lies higher than the other.
And thus you should administer this for both large wounds and smaller wounds.
And for small wounds, prepare what is necessary, and then have your most skilled assistant do this, who should hold the wound with their hand and approximate the lips of it, and then proceed to suture it carefully.
And this is the common method of suturing: take a needle, or a needle holder according to the quantity and amplitude of the wound; then lay the end of the needle between your ring finger and little finger, and tighten the needle so that you do not put thread at both ends of the suture, but rather between the two ends of the edges inside the wound, until it penetrates from one side; and once you have brought together the two ends of the suture and the two ends of the tissue, this makes four knots.
Take a needle with double thread and pass it through the wound on both sides, then bring together the two lips of the wound by sewing them up properly; then let go of your finger and put another needle in that one, and again tie this needle just as you did the first; and do not stop doing this until you have completed the whole stitching of the wound.
And the needles should be of medium fineness between coarseness and subtlety, for fine needles pierce the flesh too quickly; and thick ones are difficult to insert into the tissue, and thus it is best that they be of medium fineness and thickness.
And it is not good for the needle to be inserted at both ends of the tissue simultaneously, because this causes the flesh to be pierced too quickly and allows the wound to separate before it has consolidated.
And do not extend the stitches too far so that the wound is not hindered from consolidating.
And then cut the ends of the needles so that they do not cause irritation under the dressing; and place powder spread around the stitches, which will cover the ends of the needles; and leave them in until you know that the wound has already consolidated.
And this type of stitching with needles is most convenient for small wounds, as you can use one or two needles or around that many.
But the second type of stitching is common: it involves passing the needle through four points, two ends of the tissue and two corners of the suture, in one motion, with a needle that has thread properly wrapped around it in terms of fineness and thickness.
When you have pierced these four extremities with the needle, reduce the needle from that part which you began, so that the thread may be drawn up from the most prominent part of the wound, so that the suture may be like a seam on cloth where the pieces are tightly sewn together.
And pause between each stitch by the width of the smallest finger.
And this suture is known to be suitable for humans.
And in such a suture, I stitched the wound of a man who was injured in his abdomen with a knife, and the disruption of the wound was greater than one palm, and it had penetrated around two palms from the edge of the intestines, and the disruption was in the middle of the organ; then I reduced that after the intestine had been out of the wound for twenty-four hours.
And the wound healed in the space of fifteen days or thereabouts, and he was treated and recovered; and afterward he spoke with words for some time, and returned to his usual state.
And other doctors had judged that he could not be healed; and by miracle of fire, for I did not treat him with any drug, since it was in a place where drugs were not available nor any medicine, but I placed over the wound two pieces of cloth soaked in honey twice a day and washed it frequently with water mixed with honey until it healed.
But I will describe two types of suture according to the description in the text.
And it is that you begin the suture from the outer part and pass the needle from the outer part to the inner part.
And so when the needle penetrates into the bone and in that place twist the thread according to straightness and length of the entire body, and leave the extremity of the suture in which you have inserted the needle; and make it penetrate at the other extremity the needle from the inner part to the outer part at the other extremity of the wound.
And when you have made that penetration, now make another penetration in that same extremity of the wound from the outer part to the inner part; and leave the extremity of the suture that is in this place, and make the needle penetrate at the other extremity from the inner part to the outer part.
And make your penetration such that when you penetrate it into the suture at the extremity of the wound, which is in the part inside so that all of this penetrates.
And then start from this side and proceed to the other side at the extremity that is of the suture in the other place, and extract the needle from the bone into which it penetrates, and then reduce it back to that bone and attach the extremity of the suture that is on the other side to this extremity of the wound; and extract it from the bone that is on this side.
And do this one side and then the other, and do this alternately until you sew up everything that is outside according to that example.
And the essence of this method of suturing is that you sew with a suture thread as if sewing skins, in such a way that you sew the suture one time from the side of the bone extremity, and leave it open on another pass until it is complete.

On the treatment of abscesses and fistulas.
Know that every wound and abscess when it becomes old and chronic, and forms an ulcer, and is not consolidated or healed over time without being excised, then, regardless of which part of the body it affects, it is universally called a fistula, and we refer to it as such an abscess.
And truly, a fistula is a felt-like nodulation, hard and white, at which there is no pain but to which there is itching as there is itching from the irritation of bird's lice; and for this reason it is called such an itchy rash, that is, irritation.
And it is in some people moist, which flows continuously; and the moisture is easily excised within a few hours. And at times this moisture is thick, and it is little, and it is coarse, and it is fine.
Having shown all those causes, the signs of them, and their treatment with medicines in the division.
And it is necessary for me to tell you about the treatment of fistulas, which is performed through an operation with my method.
And you should know that fistulas can occur in all parts of the body; some of them reach large veins and arteries, or nerves, or the bladder, or intestines, or the kidney, or any of the ribs, or any of the vertebrae of the back, or any of the complex joints such as those of the hand and foot; and others are close to the main part of the limb and similar areas.
And therefore, among these fistulas that follow this method, they are among the diseases difficult to heal, and do not receive a cure easily, and dealing with them requires effort and knowledge.
And if any of the fistulas has a depth that does not reach the places mentioned above and you hope for and trust in its cure, and you wish to gain knowledge of it, then take a probe of iron or steel, and if the fistula goes straight ahead, explore it with this.
And if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a probe of thin lead, for the lightness of its body causes it to float and bend around the tortuosity.
And if the fistula is having horrible twists, it is not possible for you to signify upon it with a probe.
Then irrigate the openings through those openings, for the moisture with which you irrigate will go from that one to the other openings and flow out.
And then inquire carefully to know if there is bone or nerve here, and whether the depth of the fistula is distant or near, or if it is a fistula with many openings that exceeds your strength and your understanding, in which you investigate the malady, and the cause of the pain under your compression on the spot, and similar signs and accidental causes related to the abscess.
And when you have stood on all those causes with true judgment, then proceed to the cure with confidence: and this is that you now consider whether it is a fistula apparent near or in a soft place, distant from a joint, or nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places I have told you about.
Now you dry the fistula as was said before in its narration, and remove what is in that filtration and of corrupted flesh, and what has grown out from the opening of the flesh or of similar tissue, and treat it until it is healed.
If the fistula is of great depth and follows a straight course, it is necessary to incise that as far inward as you are able, and then remove all its corrupted flesh; afterwards apply bandages wrapped in sharp medicines, and insert these into the cavity of the fistula which you have probed with iron.
And do this for several days until the sharp medicine has dissolved all that is rotten in the cavity of the fistula from corruption.
And then treat it with applications that generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it does not heal with this treatment, then you should cauterize it as we have recommended.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and you need to ascertain this, then you should probe it and dry it as we have recommended, unless you prohibit any reason involving a vein or nerve or main limb as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed and there is corruption and blackness on it, then you should excise that until all the corruption is removed; afterwards treat it with what will generate flesh until it is healed.
And if it does not heal and pus continues to flow as before, then you will know that you have not completely eradicated the corruption; then uncover it again and carefully examine its condition and clean it with the utmost diligence, and afterwards treat it.
And if it heals, that is good; but if not, then you will know that the corruption is in the depths of the body, which you have not reached, and then in that case you should only treat the symptoms.
And if the corrupted bone is small, it is up to you to remove that; then you pull it out with fine forceps, which are suitable for this purpose.
And if there are many bones, then carefully perform the extraction of all of them, and do not leave any behind for any reason due to your strength.
But if any cause of humidity opposes you, and in their incision there is neither fear nor error, then incise those ligaments or muscles as necessary, and free the bones.
But if it is not possible for you to remove the bones at this time, then apply to the wound what will alleviate the inflammation, and leave it as it is until such time as the area around those bones has become flesh and ligaments, and guard against the wound consolidating, and ensure that the section you have incised does not contract during your operation.

But if it is a large bone such as the bone of a camel or ox or similar, and what is corrupted of it is only its surface, then scrape off that corruption with resistance only behind until that blackness and corruption are removed, and then restore the wound.
But if what is corrupted of it is a large part, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to close that area and thoroughly incise within the location where the corruption has spread, and then treat it until it is consolidated.

A young man around xxx years old came with pain in his body, and concerning the internal parts where the matter inside the body has been conveyed to the abdomen, it is a large abscess, and there is not an external cause for this.

The extremities can become diseased either through an intrinsic or extrinsic cause. And similarly, the cause of the corruption may be the sting or bite of certain venomous creatures such as a scorpion, viper, or rattlesnake and the like.

And similarly, if the corruption affects the hand, you must amputate at the wrist so that the corruption does not spread to the forearm.
And if necessary, you must amputate the arm at the elbow joint.

And similarly, if the corruption reaches the toe, you must amputate it at one of the joints.

And if the corruption has already reached the bone, then there is no remedy but dismissal, and to leave the patient to die.

Regarding the treatment of sinus conditions and the quality of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in some fleshy members and the space of the abscess extends inside to form a collection of pus, and then it is broken and perforated, and the pus is discharged from there, leaving the place empty as if it were a vessel, and the skin that covers it remains thin like cloth, and it has not progressed to corruption nor affected the bone or nerve or ligament, it is called a sinus, and it is not named fistula.
However, if the space is prolonged within so that corruption presses upon some member, it is then called a fistula and not a carbuncle.
And the treatment for it is to incise that swelling completely as it revolves, and especially when it becomes fine like cloth, and ensure that it does not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
However, if you expect that the swelling will adhere and that such corruption has not yet reached the deepest part, and there is pus in the swelling, then your treatment should be to use an enema.
And you should consider the sinus, and if it is large and the arm with which you are treating has a foul odor, then use an enema with Egyptian medicine made of oil, vinegar, and honey in equal parts, mixed together in a vessel until the mixture becomes thick like molasses.
And then take what is necessary and dissolve the medicine in water and honey; use this enema for the sinus, and secure the opening of it, leaving the medication inside for the duration of two hours.
And then extract that medicine carefully; do this for several days until the sinus is cleansed and the odor is removed.
And at times an enema of ash water is used when you do not have this medicine available; take the ashes of wheat, put them in water; then strain it and use that water for the enema until you are confident that the sinus is cleansed.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then use an enema of honey and water mixed in equal parts, and also use an enema of vinegar and honey mixed together, because honey has cleansing properties and vinegar helps to adhere the sinus, especially when the vinegar has a strong astringent and drying effect.
And when you see that some wounds are swollen and you confirm that the sinus is already drained, then irrigate them with water that promotes flesh growth.
And it is that you take a decoction of white myrtle and dissolve it with rose oil and vinegar; or you can irrigate it with various medicines and other decoctions that have the properties already described in the chapter on decoctions.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow and does not allow the cannula for irrigation to pass through, then gradually widen it with a small instrument, and pause in the dilation while wrapped in an Egyptian decoction or basilicon decoction until it is sufficiently widened.
And similarly, if the opening is too wide, then you should bring together the edges with stitches and leave it in a condition suitable for irrigation to be added.
And if the opening from which the fluid flows is raised to the upper parts, then dry it in a lower position where the fluid can flow downward, for when the arm is constricted at the depth of the sinus, flesh will be formed there.
And if it is not possible to dry the sinus in a lower position relative to the bone as you wish, then proceed to prepare the member with a method by which the fluid flows easily according to your circumstances and without any obstruction hindering the flow.
Move the one who is above the opening of the sinus, which you should drain at all times so that what is inside the arm exits. And so that you know whether what comes from inside the arm is thin or thick, or is digested or undigested.
And with this, you should consider the location of that sinus, whether in there the patient feels pain or not, and if it is in an abscess.
And as your treatment is prolonged in this manner, and you see that the matter that is discharged over the opening of the sinus has adhered or tends to adhere, then you must loosen it or do so every third day. When you loosen the crust, then change the dressing for a new one and renew the application.
But if within the sinus there is space for subtle pus to accumulate, then do not despair of its adherence, for healing will naturally occur, as some people experience delayed regeneration of flesh in their wounds, while others see the opposite happen.
And if for several days the arm remains undigested and pus is not discharged from the sinus, then you know that the sinus does not adhere. And if the adherence of the sinus is delayed and becomes prolonged, then adjust to a drying effect in your medications. If the medicines are moist in their substance and in their efficacy, they should be dried as well when there is an excess of moisture present.
And the most important thing in curing is to use a well-defined dressing and dissolve it in rose oil. Apply it over the wound as you would with an earlier dressing of its substance, and proceed accordingly.
In that action, when there is no bandage, find a dressing, honey which you have cooked so that it thickens, and use it; or take myrrh and aloes and sprinkle them, and grind over the honey which is on the fire, and immediately apply this to cloth and strengthen that upon the joint; and apply this with the honey which you have cooked so that it thickens, and sprinkle over cloth and strengthen that.

And if the joint is prolonged and has not been healed by this treatment, then you will know that the corruption is in its bone, and it affects the bones or other hard members or the ligaments; now treat it with a fistulous cure, as mentioned above.
On the treatment of paronychia and cracked nails, and the incision of an additional finger and sectioning.

On incision for inflammation of the uvula.
This illness is called in our land nafir, and it is pain that comes to certain members of the body, and it changes from one member to another.
And now I will describe it as I am about to recount to you. I was called to attend to a sick woman in a village, and when I uncovered her arm, I saw a small inflammation in the funis vein of the arm. And after it had been ruptured for about an hour, I saw that this inflammation moved just as quick as wax would rise, but more quickly than one might expect, like living silver flowing from place to place.
And then the pain moved from that location and settled in the muscle, and it stayed there for about an hour.
And then it moved to the other side of the body until it reached the other arm.
And then she told me that it was revolving throughout her body as I had observed, and I was greatly amazed by its change and the speed with which it moved from one member to another. And I had seen this before in my own patients in that manner, but I have never witnessed such pain being transferred from one member to another. But I did not see this in my patients as I did in the woman.
And I did not think it could be this way except that the woman was of weak constitution, with delicate limbs and exposed veins, in which this wind-like sensation appeared to change.
It is necessary that there does not appear according to this rear curvature in those who are delicate and of thin human veins weakened.

Of the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are varied according to their types and the parts of the body where they strike, for their diversity is such that some have large tips, others small, some concave, others blunt; and among them there are solid ones; those with three angles, and those with tongues; and those which are like spears.
And of the signs of the head are when it falls upon the arrow, and passes through the bone, and exits from the brain which is above the head, for it happens by the soda vehement, and scotoma, and vertigo, and rigor in the eyes, and inflammation and rigor of the tongue, and spasm and mixture of understanding and vomiting of bile, and easily cut off is speech, and destroyed is voice, and exits from the place of the plague moisture white like dust, and there are of it wetness to the appearance as of flesh.
And therefore if these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment and extraction of the arrow, unless it is outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it falls into the heart, so that it is near the left breast, and feels there where it is fixed in hard tissue, not soft; and easily the movement of the arrow is like the movement of the pulse, and draws black thick blood from the wound, and dries that coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat; therefore you know that death approaches soon.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung, spewing frothy blood from the wound, and the vessels near the neck are swollen, and the color of the patient changes, and he exhales great breath, and demands a craving for cold air.
And if the arrow falls on the membrane which is in the thighs, and the pain then is near the small ribs, and it causes great and violent pain and sighing, and there is no strength at all in all the members of the muscles.
And if the arrow falls on the stomach, then very quickly there will be from the wound undigested food.
And if the arrow is in the belly, and it is fixed within it, make some incision in the wound, or the tissue, or the intestine which is disrupted, and there is no hope for the cure of the arrow's removal.
And if the arrow strikes in the bladder, and it comes out with urine, and processes for some reason to the parts outside, and suddenly there is pain over the injury, then you know that it is fatal.
But the other members as well, such as in the face, the neck, and the jaw, and the shoulders and the helpers, and the vertebrae of the back, and the collarbone, and the ribs and similar parts of the limbs, are saved according to the best dispositions when the arrow does not hit a vein or artery and is not a venomous arrow.
And I recount this to you as I have seen regarding the nature of these arrows, so that you may understand through this about your own treatment.
The cause is that in a person who was struck by an arrow in the lacrimal bone near the root of the nose, I removed it from the other side, left the gray matter of the ear alone, and he healed without damage to his eye.
And I removed another arrow from a Jew who was struck in the gray matter of his eye. After leaving the eyelid intact, the arrow was already hidden and only a small part of it could be grasped to which its shaft adhered. It was a large sharp arrow of the Turks, made of iron like a lion's tooth, which had no barbs.
