On wind exposure and the qualities of using those [methods].
Wind exposures are specifically for horns, or ears, or hands.
And wind exposure is done in two ways: one involves incision and extraction of blood, and the other is wind exposure without incision.
And this wind exposure that is without incision is done in two ways, as it can be with fire or without fire.
And for the wind exposures that are administered with incision and extraction of blood, there are fourteen sites on the body. Of these, one is located at the back of the head; another is in the middle of both sides of the neck; wind exposures under the chin below the jaw and beside the mouth; wind exposures of the two cheeks; wind exposures of the throat, which is near the end of the neck; wind exposures of two armpits; wind exposures of two elbows; and wind exposures of two knees.
Wind exposure does not extract blood but rather from the small scattered veins in the flesh, and through this method, one does not capture the virtue as with phlebotomy; nor is it necessary for wind exposure to be done with fire or without fire in any illness caused by internal congestion until the entire body is evacuated.
However, if necessity requires wind exposure for illness or as a continuous treatment, administer it at any time at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, in the middle of the month, and at certain intervals.
And therefore when you encounter any of those causes, and especially if it is in the third or middle of the month, I then command bleeding to be performed two or three hours before sunset.
And the benefit of bleeding from the neck is that when you encounter heaviness in the head and sinking in the eyes; but it should be done near complete evacuation of the entire body.

Thus begins the chapter of the third part: on restoration. This chapter is considered necessary in the art of medicine; it deals with the restoration of fractures and dislocations of bones.
Oh, son, be aware that this chapter is boasted about by foolish doctors and the vulgar, and by those who have not for any length of time regarded the books of the Ancients or read a single letter from them.
And for this reason it is done that this method of the scientists in our land is kept private; and I have not forgotten it for any length of time at all; and I have not adopted what follows, but through long reading of the books of the Ancients and studying to understand them, from which I extracted the science.
And thus I directed myself to experiments and studies throughout the length of my life.

First Chapter.
A summary and collection from the treatment of fractures of bones, from those whose preliminary steps are necessary before we begin to recall the treatments of different types of fractures and their various aspects. Therefore, let us say in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of the discourse; and the chapters to which one must pay attention at the beginning in order to understand them correctly; for it is necessary that you and those who wish to learn this noble art from you know these well.
And therefore I say that when it happens to someone that they have a fracture, dislocation, torsion, or fall, at that moment you must decide at the beginning whether to perform venesection or to dress them immediately, if no cause prohibits it, such as weakness of strength, or if it is an infant who has happened to them, or an elderly person in decline, or if it is a time of intense heat or cold.
And therefore be content with food that is light, such as fresh herbs and meat of birds and small animals; and prohibit heavy meats and foods that fill the body, and all food that fills the veins with blood, until you are sure of a safe condition regarding abscesses, and if you do not fear an effusion of matter to the limb.
Then return the patient to their usual regimen as soon as they are accustomed to it.
And when the broken bone begins to be restored, now it is necessary that the patient be fed with nutritious foods that are moist and heavy, such as those made of wheat cooked with water, rice, peas, beef tripe, hens, fresh pork, and similar meats, and with this regimen the healing of the fracture occurs more easily and better.
And know that bones broken in people who are young and in the elderly cannot be repaired and consolidated according to their nature through the drying up of the limbs.
But nature adjusts and applies them around the broken bone from all sides in a manner similar to glue, where there is thickness that adheres and strengthens it as they coagulate together until they reach strength and firmness, just as fire at the beginning does not prevent it from accomplishing its work.
And for this reason it is necessary to withhold from the patient foods that are heavy and viscous and thick, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the members, for a fracture of the bone of the thigh is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the arm; and similarly all other members are different among themselves.
And I wish to remind you of every type of fracture, explained in its own separate chapter.
Note the diversity of fractures within themselves. And the types of bone fractures are diversified within themselves; for one type of arch-shaped fracture is different from those that occur in the stars; and some fractures are according to the length of the bone: and among the fractures of the shaft, splinters, and closed and open fractures, there are also fractures like when a pen is prepared for writing, and there is a fracture due to plague and disruption in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And each type has its own proper treatment in the restoration, which will be a reminder of them separately in their places.
Note the signs of a bone fracture. And by this, a bone fracture is known: there is a deformity of the bone, and from its prominence and appearance to the touch, and also when you press it with your hand.
And therefore, when there is no apparent deformity or swelling and upon gentle touch when you touch the bone with movement and the patient does not feel great pain, then there is no fracture here.
There might be a deformity or small fissure, so do not press it with your hand and with strong compression. Instead, apply one of the medicines about which there will be a reminder at an appropriate moment for what is suitable; and it may be narrow due to subtle constriction.
And know that when the place is arched and not divided into two or any other part than where it suffers from swelling, then you should begin your work with the correction of the wound that the abscess affects.
And if an abscess affects it, then leave it for some days until the abscess subsides; and afterward bandage it in whatever way you can, making it light and secure.
And know that the restoration and adjustment of the flesh is easier than that of the bone in which the swelling occurs.
And then strengthen it according to what will be remembered of it.
But if the bone is swollen, then it is necessary to extend this broken limb from both parts, whether it is a hand or a foot; or use your hand if the member is small; or two cords or one cord and your hand.
And place yourself in such a position that you can put the limb down bandaged according to its natural figure, while the parts that are extended from the broken member require you to return those addictions securely and in whatever way you can back into their places.
And be careful according to your strength that no harm comes to the patient due to your operation or injury; and strive according to your ability to apply one bone to its companion bone in their best forms.
And it is necessary that at that opportune moment you use your hand effectively, and if you find any differing cause, correct it and bandage it according to the extent of your skill.
And from the excessive extension and compression, so do some foolish doctors cause an abscess through their operation, or they cause contraction in the limb as we have seen this happen due to their foolish operations.
And the quality of the restriction of the tranquil limb is what I recommend; for you to know that broken limbs vary in their smallness and greatness and in the form of those.
And for those that are small like the arm and the finger and the wrist and similar to these, then it is necessary that the wrapping be of soft fine cloth.
But for those that are large like the thigh or the chest, it is necessary to wrap them in linen and hard material, because the linen wrapping connects the large limb and strengthens it evenly from all sides, thus preventing any impurity from entering.
And appropriately begin, with proper alignment of the evacuation, to pause gradually according to this, on the site of the fracture with three wrappings or four depending on what the limb requires; and strengthen it gently with your hand along the bandaging.
And apply to it the most sovereign poultice for the place of the fracture, and bind it gently so as not to restrict the area where you have bound the place of the fracture. And apply an extension to the wrapping of the fracture site for a short period, and gradually loosen the bandage until you have removed most of it.
And then take another bandage and wrap it around the fractured site over the previous wrapping, and afterwards apply to it the dressings below the fracture; and let your operation be in the tightening of the dressing and in the loosening of it as we have said about the first dressing above.
And then place between the dressings a piece of leather or cloth on which the curvature of the fracture is modeled, if there is any curvature; and if not, do not place anything in that area.
And then place another bandage over it, and afterwards apply properly made splints over these dressings if there is no swelling or abscess in the area, and now place on what causes the abscess and reduces the swelling; and leave this for several days; and now bind the splints over it.
And let the splints be about half a cubit long and carefully crafted; or they can be made of willow wood; or from palm strips; and let the width of each splint be according to this form and figure:
However, the splint placed over the fracture should be slightly thicker and longer than the others, and the length of the splint according to how it fits the limb in size; and then bind over the splints with another bandage as per your first binding; and afterwards cover it above properly as we have said about binding, and this binding should be tighter near the fracture site, and as it moves away from the fracture, the binding should become looser.
And let the threads be moderate in thickness and fineness, and let them be of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen some foolish paunches use cords of twisted thread for their sewing, it is a great error, when you see the stitches not lying straight.
And also very fine threads are not suitable, for you will not be able to achieve with them what you want by sewing, and it is necessary that there be more than a finger's width between stitch and stitch.
And if the patient is suffering from pain at the ends of the ribs near the stitches in those places, then pause and use silk or carded wool between that and the skin so that it does not cause injury.
Sometimes a fracture occurs due to a blow, and a disruption happens in the collarbone, and I will recall this case in the chapter dedicated to its uniqueness.
And know that not all broken members should be set with splints on the first day; the reason for this is that when the member is large, it should not be placed upon splints immediately but rather after two or three days or more, according to your certainty about the danger of suppuration.
And this is the method of the plasters with which the Ancients treated fractures, dislocations, and twists, which were applied over such injuries.
Method of making a plaster for the rapid restoration of fractures, suitable for delicate individuals and especially for women and children, as it does not attract heat or irritation: take powdered flour, which is the fine part that adheres to the sides of the mill during grinding.
And confine it as it is, but sift it with white cloth, and pause the compounding of it not thin nor subtle; and then use that.
Another method for plasters to treat fractures, dislocations, and twists: recipe of asafetida, of ledanum, of acacia, of inula, and migraach, which is the root of wild millet, of each a drachm x; of myrrh and aloes one drachm v; alchaichol two drachms xx; all should be dried, sifted, and mixed with watered alchaichol or with white cloth if the composition of the mixture is too hot; and then use this plaster, for it has a good composition and quickly restores broken bones, and helps many people.
Another method for a plaster to treat fractures and twists: recipe of migraach (root of wild millet) and extract of white alum, one drachm x; myrrh and aloes one drachm v; acacia six drachms vi; Armenian bole two drachms xx.
All should be triturated in a mortar with a pounding tool, and then sifted, and compounded with water or white cloth if the mixture is too thick, and it should be administered.

Method of preparing a poultice to administer for the restoration of a bone fracture and when you wish to resolve the remnant of an abscess: take one part each of marshmallow root, chamomile flowers, violets, and flour of barley; mix all these finely and cook them in water, provided that the limb is not inflamed.

However, Galen is seen to have applied poultices to the broken limb during its restoration and for causes such as slight drying with a bit of heat, like myrrh, onions, and similar to those causes which we have mentioned.
Method of preparing a poultice to relieve injury and pain: take equal parts of linseed, chickpeas, chopped human hair or bird's down, marshmallow root, and salt; mix these finely and sieve them, then cook them together to make a poultice.
The quantity according to which it is necessary that the bandage remain and then be removed is such that you should consider whether pain does not occur or progress nor is the bone broken from its place; therefore, do not untie it for several days. And if intense pain, swelling, or inflammation occurs at the site, then you should be cautious or loosen it at that hour, and remove the plaster.

And afterwards repeat that gentle compression and small plaster without making it as tight as your first one, and gradually do this until it is healed.
And if you find that the abscess and hardness and pain and swelling and all other symptoms have subsided completely, and you need a plaster and compression, then repeat that as you did at the beginning, gradually.

Chapter Second: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and the causes are distinct. Some fractures are caused by the impact of a blunt object, where the impact displaces all the bone inward, which is behind the bone, just as it would in wood, and thus this type is called an "ascial" fracture; or a sharp object cuts some part of the bone and cuts through the flesh but does not pass the cut inward to the back of it; and this type of fracture is called absolute elevation.
This is about the injury of these two fractures, either it is great or small, and of the fracture that is comminuted or crushed; and the cause of them is percussion by a weak object or falling on a stone, and similar things.
And this fracture is penetrating, which already approaches the panniculus, which is below the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the injury of these two fractures is either great or small.
And of the fracture that is weakened in the bone, in the subtlety of the hairline, and for this reason this particular type of fracture is called a capillary fracture.
And it is a fracture that is caused either by falling or by the impact of a stone and similar things, which causes some lamina of the bone to enter into its inner parts, and it occurs in the concave location just as it happens with ribs when they suffer an impact and break upon their inward entry.
And this is more commonly done in the bones of children, just as it happens with the heads of infants.
And for all these types of fractures, treatments are used, which are remedies applied separately. And I will return to the recall of all this in its place.
And you will recognize all these types of fractures by examination on them and by inquiry into their painful areas and the appearance of corrupted flesh that is on them.
And the capillary fracture is recognized by this: it appears on the bone, and when it is cleaned, it is treated over the painful area because the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of fractures consider at first the symptoms of the patient, and if you see through these symptoms that it signifies by clear indication great fear, as is vomiting from anger, and stretching out, and loss of understanding, and hoarseness of voice, and fainting, and acute fever, and such things of the limbs and stiffness of them, and similar to those of the symptoms in the major dispositions, without doubt he will not be healed.
And if you see unusual symptoms that give hope of health, then you begin the treatment of the fracture.
When the cause is such that it has come to you as a complaint at the beginning as it is in this case and this is during the winter days, then it is necessary for you to study the condition of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all dispositions.
And if it is during the summer days, then it is necessary for you to expose the bone before the seventh day, because what is next to the bone may become corrupted by pus, and such symptoms as we have mentioned may occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the membrane covering the brain and is in small fragments and abrasions, then it is necessary for you to incise the fragmented and abraded part as I will tell you.
And you should shave the head of the patient who is affected, and uncover the bone according to custom and method as best you can according to the shape of the injury, so that it is clear on the patient.
And if it happens to you that you need to detect the bone, or there is bleeding or an abscess requiring cautery, then you should prepare for what is necessary, and that is to apply the area with cloths infused in wine and oiled cloth, and let the wound rest until the abscess subsides and bleeding stops; then begin circumferential treatment of the bone and its removal.
In water let it be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you incise the bone with an incisor of such size, and this is its shape: And then apply another incisor, this one slightly wider than the previous; and this is its shape:
And then apply an even wider incisor than the second one.
And it is necessary to have various incisors, some wider than others and some shorter than the rest; they should be sharp at both ends, and made of iron or steel.
And use them with ease in percussion on the incisors so that you do not jar the head and injure it.
And if the bone is hard and strong, it is necessary to perforate around it before you apply the incisors along with the chisels, which are called non-deep chisels.
And they are called this only because these do not reach the inner part of the skull bone when used around it; thus, the chisel has a rounded end or margin on top, similar to an edge or small circle that prevents it from penetrating too deeply into and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take many of these chisels, each suited to the thickness of each bone, so that you have at hand for all parts of the skull an end that is sharp, with the quantity in length and width according to the amount of thickness of this part.
This is the form of three kinds of trephines: The quality of piercing in the circuit of the broken bone is that you place the trephine on the bone and revolve it with your hand until you know that the bone has been penetrated; then change the trephine to another location, and continue this process throughout the extent of the thickness of the ray or around about that.
And then incise with an incisor that which is between each piece of the bone, and do it as finely as you can at the end of lightness, as we have said, until you eradicate the bone, or with your hand, or by any other cause through some instruments that you have prepared for this purpose, such as probes and fine forceps.
And it is necessary that you guard with all caution that the incisor does not touch any part of the brain.
And so when you have perforated the bone in a circuit, and it will be separated from the brain if it continues at that point, and you will extract it; then rasp and smooth that in the roughness of the place where it is in the bone with another instrument similar to an incisor, but it must be more thin and finer than other incisors.
And if any part of the small bones remains and causes trouble, now take that with ease using what is appropriate from your instruments; and then treat the wound with lint and dressings as we have mentioned.
And the other method of operation is light, distant from fear, which Gualia calls and the leaf praises greatly as a praiser; and this is the speech on it: It is necessary for you at the beginning to start uncovering the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe and most apparent, until when you have uncovered that part and you have placed below it the extremity of this incisor lent and cheerful whose form is as follows:
And it is that the lens-shaped part is lion and does not cut any cause, and the acute part is of it in two sides frontwards in length, as you see; that the lens-shaped part be adjusted to the ciphac with the face of it cutting acute on the bone.
And for this reason, you strike upon the saw of a part with a small hammer so that you cut through all the bone with ease as it revolves, and be sure concerning the flap that no accident happens to it in any way, and if the operator were the most foolish of all men and the least knowledgeable among them; certainly, and even if he were asleep.
Now if there is any cause attached to the bone of the flap in some places of bone, it will cut that which adheres to the end of the saw at the lens-shaped part, and it would deliver that which adheres with ease, for it is delivered from harm and fear.
And if the bone fracture does not penetrate into the flap, and that which is excised or remains of the bone is its surface, and it remains with roughness and small protrusions, now you should scrape away that roughness and eradicate those protrusions with fine razors for which you have prepared various quantities so that it is possible for you to administer those most suitable in every place and the most appropriate of them according to how your operation on that broken bone and its shape directs you.
And you should administer in your proportion at which you scrape the bone at the beginning with the widest of those razors, and then gradually use one increasingly finer, and do not cease doing this continuously until you reach administering the thinnest and finest of all of them.
In each of the other small fractures and subtle fractures, you should administer care according to what has been lost for the purpose of correction.
And it is because she is not intimidated by any of those who have studied in that art. And this is about what we have written concerning large fractures.
And if the fatty tissue is rectangular when you remove the bone, now you should take a piece of cloth according to the quantity of the wound, and soak that in warm oil, and place it over the opening of the wound; then take another folded or triple-layered cloth and soak it in vinegar and warm oil, and place it over the first cloth; do this as lightly as possible so that you do not press on the swelling; then apply a wide bandage to it, but do not bind it too tightly except just enough for the wound.
And leave it with this treatment for one day or two until you are sure of the absence of abscess; then unbind it and administer medicines that are suitable for excessive dryness, such as lint poultices and flour mixtures, and powders of iris and aristolochia, and similar ones.
And it is that you should make these medicines into powder that is finely ground and applied to the wound as dry powders; generally, administer any medicine whose property is to cleanse without causing irritation.
And be careful according to your virtue that the wound in your treatment remains clean; do not allow it to become infected or suffer from any other issues; and do not let its virulence increase. For when it is properly placed over the fatty tissue of the brain, it corrupts that tissue and weakens it, leading to further illness due to such malignancy.
And it happens in the skull where the bone has been cleaned and especially when the treatment of it is delayed, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore consider the hours if such accidents happen to the patient, so that you know it is dangerous without any doubt.
And if it is not blackness but the medicine that has been applied above which causes this darkness, then you should take one part of honey and three parts of olive oil, and with these wet a cloth, and then place that on the skull; and afterwards treat it with the spices for healing until it is healed.
Chapter III.
On the restoration of the nose when it is cut.
Know that the nose is not broken except into two parts above mostly, or one of those sometimes, because there are two bones in it, as the lower part is cartilaginous and does not break, and only swelling, distortion, and curvature happens to it.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then you should insert your finger into the side of the nose, and adjust that fracture with your middle finger inside and support it with your thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this with ease, and be careful that your operation does not cause pain to the patient.
And if the fracture is in the uppermost part of the nose, and your finger cannot reach it, then you should equalize it at the end of the rod which is slightly thick.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then make it look like this.
And if you can perform the restoration on the first day of the fracture, do so; and if not, then on the seventh or tenth day, proceed with the rest when the swelling subsides.
And immediately insert into the end of the nose a plug of linen cloth if the fracture is in one piece, or place two cloths if the fracture is in two pieces, and make sure that the cloth is thick enough to fill the cavity of the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers say that the cloths should be soaked in boiled honey, and changed every day, but I have not seen this myself; however, it is advisable to soak the plug in albumen of cooked eggs mixed with powder of rue; and then leave the cloth inside until the bone is set and the cartilage has hardened.
And sometimes a piece of wax is inserted into the nose instead of cloth, wrapped in linen cloth; and this should be done when the fracture of the nose is very severe and the patient is prohibited from inhaling.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do it this way, and if you wish, use cloths.
And if another swelling occurs, apply a plaster to the nose with cerate or wax soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or with honey water.
But if no swelling occurs, then it is advisable to apply a plaster outside with sesame flour and powdered herbs mixed with egg white; and then place it over the injured area, and do not cover the nose with anything.
If bones of the nose are broken in a small fracture or crushed, then at that time it is necessary to cut around them and extract them with instruments suitable for this; and promptly close the fracture, and treat it with substances that heal and strengthen, and with plasters appropriate for this.
And if it happens that there is a swelling inside the nose, then at that time it is necessary to treat this with poultices and administer lead tubes until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
On the restoration of the jaw when it is dislocated.
When the lower jaw is broken and there is no swelling at the fracture site, then check if the fracture is only on the surface and not split into two parts with curvature inside, in this case its diagnosis is easy.
And you must, if the fracture is on the right side, insert your left finger into the mouth of the patient; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert your right finger; and push the swelling of the fracture from inside with ease to the outside parts, using your other hand from the outside part of the bone; do this gently and steadily.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two parts, then you must administer extension of both parts according to straightness as much as possible until they are aligned.
And if there is a concussion of the teeth leading to disruption, then strengthen those you expect will break with wire or silver or thread; and promptly place on the broken jaw some wax, and then place over it a double cloth, and place over that a large piece of clean cloth, or a pad of softened dough equal in length to the jaw; and then bind it tightly according to how your bandaging equipment is prepared and suits the application, without causing destruction.
And he commands the patient to use rest and tranquility, and to stop eating liquid foods.
And if you see that the shape has already been altered in any way, then on the third day you should examine and loosen it; afterwards, correct what has already been altered with powder of myrrh, egg albumen, or flour of linseed, after you have stopped the cerement from it, and place upon the dressing a piece of linen.
And then when it stands attached and adjusted that plaster upon it, and the position of the bone is not altered, leave that, so do not loosen it until it is healed and the fracture strengthened; for often this fracture is strengthened in three weeks.
And if on another occasion an abscess occurs, then administer what we have said often in the treatment of it until it is healed, and let that abscess be drained.
And if the fracture is with a splinter, then consider whether it is a piece of bone separated or embedded, and carefully extract those splinters with what is necessary for their removal using instruments.
And if the opening of the splinter is narrow, then enlarge it with a scalpel according to the amount necessary.
And then when you have stopped those splinters, and if they cannot be removed, close the opening of the splinter if it is wide; otherwise, place upon that one of the plasters which are suitable for it and promote healing there, until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
On the restoration of the collarbone when it is fractured.
Most often the collarbone is broken in the part in front of the muscle, and that fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it is either broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without affecting the articulation; and it is easy to restore it. Or it happens in such a way that it affects the articulation, and its restoration is difficult. Or it is such that the fracture is along the flat surface.
And the procedure in that case, when the fracture is along the flat surface, is to have two assistants present, so that one holds the supporting instrument and guides the broken collarbone, while the other extends the neck towards that part.
And similarly, adjust the fracture within the bone so that its shape is formed as needed, and there should be neither a small prominence nor a depression in it.
And if you need more extension, then place below the armpit a pad made of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to your necessity; extend and raise the collarbone and compress the pad with your hand until you adjust the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the end of the collarbone to the outer parts, so that it reaches the preon, then have the patient lie supine on their neck, and place a pad about halfway in size over the muscle.
And the assistant presses the muscle from below so that the bone of the collarbone is raised to reach the preon, and then you correct the fracture and adjust it as needed.
If you feel pain from the fork and its points, now it is necessary to cut around them and extract those pricks easily.
And if it is stuck in the bone, now you must devise an incision with one of the cutters that you have prepared for this purpose, since you have paused below the fork the instrument that serves as a reamer; and this tool is made of wood or iron, and here is its shape:
And it resembles the cup from which this is the concavity; and its width should be according to what you need for it, depending on the size of the bone or the space available.
However, the length of it should be as much as is possible for the operation; it should have two ends as you see fit, and one end should be wide while the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound that you have incised when extracting the splinter from the bone is wide, and you are sure it may form an abscess, now you should approximate the two lips of the wound with stitches.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear it may form an abscess, now you should cover the wound with dressings and powders according to the size of the wound's opening.
And if an abscess does occur, now apply a dressing soaked in vinegar and rose oil and place it over that area.
And do not let the splint press down on the broken bone; and therefore you should see the patient every day, so that when the bandaging is loose or you see that the splint has already been moved, then you must correct this and tighten the bandaging.
And place the patient's head on the pillow at the neck, and place below it the bolsters so that during sleep his head is slightly raised; thus the support of the shoulder blade will be raised, and therefore the fracture of the collarbone will be elevated by the muscle; or bind his arm to his neck.
And do not remove the bandage unless it is necessary due to an abscess or other condition within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you see that his face is suitable for it, and adjust it to the constriction and restore it so that the fracture of the collarbone may be set and healed.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the measure in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some people it is done in less time.
Chapter VI.
On the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The scapula is broken in the middle part and not at its margins.
And when it is broken at that location — and it is broken in the middle part of it — and this is known only by palpation, then according to the shape of the fracture prepare yourself and reduce it according to its natural figure in every way you can manage.
Here is the translation:
If you place the blade in one of the concavities on the spatula, then wrap that concavity with a lion's paw and proceed to push the blade according to equality.
And then apply pressure above with the metallic strip properly constricted and with firmness of the blade such that it does not move from its place.
And check the bandage each day; and whenever it is softened, change it on another day and tighten it if the blade has moved from its place.
And place the malady over its proper side.
And the spatula is restored in 20 days or in 25 days.
And when this number is completed, then you remove the bandage, and be sure that these are bones for which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed to the place of the broken bone and place the bandage there, then you cut above it, remove it, and act as we have said for the fracture of the furcula or in case of an abscess that may occur here.
Chapter VII.
On the restoration of a fractured foot.
The foot sometimes is broken in the middle and remains intact, but the extremities move as cicadas do and are comminuted.
And when the middle of the foot is broken, the symptoms are that it tilts towards the inner parts and there is great pain, difficulty in movement, coughing, fever, vomiting blood; and a cavity forms in the broken bone and is not hidden from sensation.
For the restoration of the spine, lay the patient on their back and place a bolster between their shoulders; then press against the muscles, and adjust the ribs with two fingers, and delicately perform the operation and the correction in every possible way that is suitable for this, until the shape of the bone returns as it should.
Then place the plaster and tow on it, and put a thin layer of linden bark or flax or something similar in lightness, after which you wrap that with cloth; then carefully bind that around the broken bone without putting too much pressure; and proceed to bandage following the roundness of the back several times, and tighten that moderately; then examine the bandage at all times, and when it softens, strengthen that.
And if necessity requires the removal of that due to corrosion or swelling, or pain, or an abscess that occurs in the area, then you should cut and loosen that, remove the plaster, and correct what has happened according to the method of treatment; then reapply the plaster if it is necessary for that; and adjust the binding until it is healed.
Chapter VIII.
On the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that fractures of the ribs do not occur except in thick areas where they meet; and at their extremities in front, compression does not affect them because they are cartilaginous there. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the sensation during the examination of the aforementioned areas.
And so the restoration should be such that it equalizes the fracture according to the said method as much as possible, entering where necessary, and then apply a plaster and strengthen the broken bone with a splint if that is required.
And if the fracture of the ribs inclines towards the inner parts, it often causes severe pain and puncture-like sensations, just as those who have wept experience, and because of this, the bone places a covering over itself; and there is difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And already the Ancients were skilled in that with skillful words.
And what they say is that one should avoid foods for the patient that generate inflammation and gas, so that the belly does not become swollen and distended, and the fracture is not pushed towards the outer parts.
But we should do this to ensure it does not become a light cause for generating a pus-filled abscess if one does not occur; and if it already has occurred, then this helps in dealing with it.
And some of them say that it should be paused from wind-increasing foods and afterwards squeezed with strength; and this is similar to reasoning, but it is feared that the wind will draw the superfluities to the place due to weakness.
And some of them say: the place should be covered with wool that has been submerged in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs where it is full, so that the bandage is equal when wrapping it according to its roundness; afterwards, the patient should be treated for pleurisy with diet and medicine.
And if the patient is strongly constrained by the pain which they cannot endure, and the bone has punctured through the dressing, we fear for the patient; at that time it is necessary to cut across the location, uncover the broken edge, and immediately place over that instrument which will preserve the splinter whose preservation was previously mentioned, and cut the bone with ease, and extract it.
And immediately adjust the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with stitching, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs internally, then apply poultices of powdered herbs in warmed oil and place over the area, and cure the patient with what is appropriate for an internal abscess.
And have the patient lie on their back on the side where they sleep until they are healed.
Chapter IX.
On the restoration of fractures of the spine in the back and neck.
In some cases, there is fracture of the neck bones, and slowly they heal, and similarly with the spine in the back. And when such an occurrence happens to someone and you want to know if they will heal or not, then consider this: if you see their hands already softened and numb and dead, and they cannot move them or stretch them, and when you press on them with your fingers or pierce them with a needle and there is no feeling of pain in them, then you know that they will not heal, and in such conditions it is fatal.
And if they move those parts and feel constriction when pressed on them or pierced with a needle, then you know that the bone in the neck is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see the vertebrae of the neck like this and want to know whether it will heal or not, then consider the condition.
Now if you see that the patient is already softened, and it happens like this in most cases, and suddenly when he lies on his back, windiness and involuntary evacuation occur, and sometimes he is pushed onto his back and wants to urinate but cannot, then you will know that it is fatal, and at that point do not labor in his cure.
But if none of those causes occur, the condition is better and more remote from danger.
And the treatment for him is to apply poultices to reduce the abscess until you place over the protruding vertebra olive oil that has been rubbed, or two eggs mixed and applied; place this on it several times a day until the abscess is reduced.
And now apply one of the comforting and restorative plasters, and bind it on that area; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility and not to lie on the side where pain is felt until he is healed.
And if it happens that the bone is disjointed or for some cause has become separated from it, then you should set it and put the bone in place; and then approximate the two lips of the wound, using sutures if necessary; and then treat it with one of the ointments to promote healing until it is healed.
But if the end of the bone is broken at the joint, and it is the end of the shaft, then you should insert your finger into the backside, and align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible by second intention; and then apply a plaster and bandage to it, if you need them, and bind it.
And if you feel that stitches are torn in it, then at once remove them, and treat the wound with the treatment that has been described until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
On the restoration of the broad area.
The bones of the broad areas are easily broken, and if they break, then their fracture is only communication at the ends of those bones and they split along their length and deviate to the sides; and this causes pain in the location and puncture, and movement is impaired when there is a fracture.
And the restoration of it is that you place your hand upon it until it is in the position corresponding to the figure of the fracture.
And if the fracture is at the end of it only, then you must set that according to what seems appropriate to you by equalization until it resembles its natural shape.
But if the fracture is in the long part or one that has already deviated to the sides, then you make the patient lie on their back until you are prepared for the restoration of that fracture. And when you have set that, apply a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or cane upon it, and bind it tightly with bandages so that there is no fear of puncture of the fracture or movement of the splint.
And set the concavity with what fills it until that takes the shape according to equality, and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or side.
If an inflamed abscess occurs after cauterization, then at that moment you must stop the extension of it and its restoration until the cauterized abscess is calmed, and wait with what was previously done; and then return to the restoration of it, and strengthen it as is fitting.
And if you encounter them in the bone or they are crushed, then at that moment you must treat them.
Chapter XI.
On the restoration of the fracture of the adjuncts.
The adjuncts are what lie between the ribs and the shoulder head.
And if it is fractured, then the restoration of it is in one of two ways, one of which is that you take a flat splint of moderate thickness according to this form:
And let them be attached at both ends with two straps, and then it should be suspended from a high place.
And let the patient lie on a mattress; and then place the broken arm over the splint until it reaches the midpoint where the curvature of the splint is attached.
And then suspend something heavy on that, or help him extend it to the lower parts; and then the physician should keep the fracture in place with his hands, so that the fracture returns as is necessary.
And the other way is that the patient lies supine on their neck and suspend the arm at the neck with straps; and then command two assistants, one of whom holds what is above the fracture firmly; and the other holds what is below it; and each extends it in their own direction.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then tie one bandage across the fracture and another perpendicular to it, and each of the two assistants should pull on their respective sides.
And if the fracture is close to the muscle's extremity, then place one half of the bandage over the button (or knot) and the other below the fracture, crossing it.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the joint, then place the bandage over that location and across that joint.
Wrap it with ease and effort, and as soon as it is wrapped as needed and properly applied, then secure it if no abscess occurs.
But if an abscess does occur, then loosen the bandaging within seven days, and place over it wool soaked in vinegar and rose water until the abscess subsides, and then secure it.
And the method of securing it is to place a plaster over the fracture, and then wrap a wet cloth around the plaster; and then adjust the arm with the aid of an assistant, and have the hand placed on its respective muscle.
And wrap the cloth and secure around the assistant the arm so that the arm is in a position of stability, unless some cause from the broken bone prohibits it.
And now use splints.
And place on the fracture that splint which is thicker and stronger than the others; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the break with an addition of three fingers from both sides.
And upon this draw lines along the stricture which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your stricture in the place of the fracture be much narrower. And the longer the fracture is, the smaller the stricture should be.
And thus when you have positioned the bones and the stricture as we have said, at the moment when you restore the limb to its position, then do it.
And if you fear an abscess, then at that moment leave the stricture and the bones as we have said until the seventh day.
And then consider the bandaging in all parts for three days so that no swelling or inflammation occurs in the area; or food should not reach the limb due to excessive tightness of the stricture; and thus correct everything as we have said.
But if you are confident that no cause for this will occur, then do not loosen the bandage until after three days or more.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their arms be placed on their stomach.
And place a cushion filled with wool under the helper, and at all times of the night or day ensure that the figure of the broken limb is not loose or dissolved, or that the bandaging is too slack; therefore, correct this according to your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we mentioned before, so that the food is light at first until the bandaging begins; and then it is appropriate to make the diet heavier.
As to the custom of the assistants and of the dressing of the wound is that they be changed in forty days; and at this time it should be loosened, bath given, and treated with medicines which correct it.
And if the fracture is gritty, then you do not loosen the bandage from it for one day or even two months.
Chapter XII.
On the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called the two ulnae; of which one is small and it is that which lies under the little finger, and the other is large and it is that which lies below the small one, towards the lower part.
And if the greater or lesser ulna is broken alone, or both are broken together.
And when the lesser ulna above is broken, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is quicker.
And when the ulna below is broken, the fracture of it is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the lesser ulna above, then it is appropriate for the physician, during its restoration, to allow a slight extension with ease until it is set straight.
But if the greater ulna is the one that is broken, then it is necessary to allow more vigorous extension.
And if both falanges are being cut, then at this hour you must apply the traction more forcefully.
And you must place the figure of the hand during its restoration and extension spread upon a cushion, with the wrist above all parts, so that all other fingers are in their natural position, and the thumb be lower than the others, and the patient lie reclining on a bed.
And let there be a cushion in front of him at his height or straight ahead, so that the patient does not have to strain.
And let one attendant extend the arm from below toward the patient's hand or bandage, and another attendant extend it similarly from above.
And let the physician then guide the bone into place as closely as possible according to its proper alignment.
And if in the process of setting the bone you encounter resistance, then with your skill reduce all parts to their proper place.
And if there appears in the resistance a separation that suggests the joint is affected and does not give you hope for its restoration, then proceed with it as we have described above and remove it according to the method mentioned.
But if the fracture is due to plague, I have already treated this in a separate chapter, and therefore follow the treatment described there.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then you should line a cloth with cerote made from rosin oil and white wax, which is midway between thickness and subtlety, and tightly bind it around so that the abscess subsides; then remove the cerote and apply a plaster prepared from powdered herbs mixed with egg white, and afterward place bandages; let the bandage placed over that fracture be wider and more firmly applied.
And know that the number of stars on the arm can be seen in major dispositions, whether it is a fracture in one fossa or in two fosse simultaneously.
And appropriately apply pressure to the site of the fracture firmly and vigorously, and when more force is required above or below the fracture, apply the pressure slightly less forcefully according to how it was preceded in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that you wrap around the fracture should be soft and moistened, not hard or rough, and the bandage with which it is strengthened should be of medium thickness between fine and coarse, as we have recommended.
And consider the limb and the bandaging after several days. And if any cause arises for which correction is necessary, such as swelling that occurs in the limb, you should immerse the limb in hot water until the swelling subsides.
And allow the limb to not be constrained until it rests, and then reapply the bandaging.
And if the bandaging has already been loosened and the bone is already realigned and appears to be in a similar condition, then correct it with your skill.
And consider carefully if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive bandaging, for then you should loosen it slightly and leave it for several days until nourishment reaches it, and then strengthen it.
And if the patient does not experience any of the causes we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to remove the bandaging for about 20 days or thereabouts.
And so the hand of the patient is suspended by its upper part of motion and agitation, and make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in xxx days or in xxxii days, or easily restored in xxviii days, and all of this depends on the condition of the tissues and the condition of the bone.
Chapter XIII.
On the restoration of a fracture of the thumb and of the said bones.
Know that to the thumb of the hand and to the joints of the said bones, when they are fractured, nothing happens but healing in several weeks.
And when a fracture or injury occurs to the hand, it is now necessary for the patient to lie flat and facing upward according to equality, and place the hand upon that extended.
And then the minister extends the broken bones and the physician aligns them until they are properly set.
And now it is necessary to apply plaster and cotton, unless an abscess or cauterization occurs; and then place a bandage over it according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped that in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is in the parts inside the palm, and now make it appear as a sphere of cloth, and command the patient to hold that with their fractured palm; then bind it tightly with cloth.
And let the bandage be made of soft cloth that is raised, so that the palm can apply to the wound; and bind it as needed.
If the fracture is in the parts above, place one splint on top and another under the part in the palm, so that it may be open and straight; and then bind it tightly, as if turning the hand around between the fingers with bandages.
And if it happens that there is a fracture in one of the bones of the said fingers, then place the finger as it fits and bind it to the palm, and if you wish, use a small straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not injured.
If the fracture is in other fingers, such as in the middle finger, index finger, ring finger, or little finger, then align and bind it to its corresponding finger; or bind them all continuously, which is better; or place a small splint over it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration and afterwards, the disposition of abscesses and opposing conditions — during the operation — as well as what is necessary when any cause of them arises, according to how it is described.
Chapter XIV.
On the restoration of a fracture of the collarbone.
The collarbone, which is often fractured, is evident by touch according to how it has shifted forward and upward.
And the restoration is done in such a way that one bandage is tightly wrapped above the fracture and another below it, and the patient lies prone on their belly and face.
Let the attendant extend the bandage according to its quality when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
If it extends towards the root of the spine, then the bandage should be applied to the front at the part above the navel, so that its extension stops there, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture extends towards the navel, then the bandage should be applied above the navel, and its extension should be to the parts below.
And the physician should align the bone with both hands and reduce it according to its natural appearance, so that the bone is properly joined; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs, then you should leave it alone for several days until the abscess has subsided, and then return to your treatment.
The bandaging should be done as follows: wrap a firm bandage over the fracture and fold it two or three times, pressing out any excess; then wrap the entire limb so that it goes to the ankle and the root of the spine.
And insert a long strip of cloth between the spine and the limb near the navel at the lower parts, and let the ends of the cloth extend from the highest part of both sections, then wrap around the limb and the spine any excess bandage material.
And then apply strips of cloth over the spine at the location of the fracture, and place one strip directly over the bone of the limb.
And fill the void between the bowl and the container with soft cloths, so that the constriction may be coagulated.
And then begin the binding tightly from above at the site of the fracture with three or four layers, and as you move away from the ligatured area of the fracture, your binding should be looser and lighter and softer.
And then extend the two ends of the bandage to the sides below until it reaches the nail of the toe, and secure both ends of the bandages on the other side so that the ligature is not moved from its place.
And then place the bandage over it in such a way that corrosion, abscess, swelling, or similar conditions do not occur to the limb; and if any of these causes should arise, immediately remove it and correct what has happened as soon as possible, as we have said before.
But if there is a piece of bone that needs to be placed in the bone, then you should try to set it as best as possible; and if not, cover it and move it, and treat it according to what was mentioned before until it heals.
And in some cases, this fracture of the bone is strengthened by binding it with bandages, as we have said regarding the assistant and the arm.
However, in the restoration of the limb in such a manner performed, a bad limp should not occur; and if it is restored alone or with the application of another bandage, then it is necessary that the person will have a limp permanently.
And know that the wound heals in forty days or a few months, and all this depends on the diversity of temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter XV.
On the restoration of a fractured wrist bone.
Know that the fracture of the wrist bone often occurs, but twists usually happen more frequently.
But if it is a fracture, at that moment it is merely a crack or a bruise in its parts, and this is either one pain or several pains, and all of this depends on the sensation.
And the restoration of it is such that what is broken in its parts must be equalized, and what is separated must be joined together as much as possible by adjustment, ease, and fabrication.
And then apply a plaster and place it over that round bandage if you need to use one; and bind it above strictly as is appropriate.
And then consider all the conditions we have recommended for other fractures, just as in cases of abscess and similar issues, ensure that everything opposes every accident in such a way that it is appropriate for healing.
Chapter XVI.
On the restoration of a fractured thigh bone.
Know that in the thigh there are two bones, one of which is thick and is called the femur; the other is slender and is called the fibula.
And it happens with some of the types of fracture that happen to both bones of the arm, and by this restoration is made as with the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation of it is one.
And if you break both bones together, the curvature is converted in all directions.
And if the more delicate bone is broken, the curvature is converted to the forward parts.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it below, and it is of those causes that you are not accustomed to.
And immediately administer extension and correction and fixation of the fragments according to that method.
But be careful, if the fracture of the bone is jagged with many edges, that the extension be gentle and slower, and rely on your skill.
And when there is any cause for operation on the arm, and when you align the fragments and complete the entire procedure, now you will take pieces of wood from plants that are rooted below causes that are placed in soil; make them similarly from palm branches; attach these to those with slight grooves, and they should not be of the grasping kind, and their length should be according to the length of the bone, and from the end to the most distant part.
And then wrap around each one a cloth in two layers according to the length of it, and place one at the part of the bone on one side and the other on the opposite side, and they should be from the end to the lowest location of the body.
Place the two stakes in three places at both extremities and at the middle place, for with this structure the camber is forbidden to lean towards the right and left, and it stands in good position.
And now a beam of wood is administered according to the length of the arch; and let the arch be placed in such a way that it preserves its freedom of movement.
And especially do this in the case of a fracture, and when it is properly a case of splinting.
And each day consider the arch and study it based on previous studies, and you must guard it from abscesses and inflammation or other similar causes; and when any such cause occurs, then act upon it as is necessary until it is healed.
Chapter XVII.
Of the fracture of the shinbone and of the bones of the foot and of the aforementioned.
A complete fracture does not usually occur in the shinbone; but fractures can occasionally occur in the bones of the foot; and among those of the foot, few suffer fractures, and they rarely experience anything more than strain due to improper positioning.
And if the bones of the foot suffer a fracture and those bones are elevated above each other, then the patient should place their foot on prepared ground as mentioned.
And then place the foot on what is elevated by those bones, and proceed to press them, and bind them until they return to their proper places.
And then apply a plaster and place padding above it, and below the arch of the foot, place a small board with two padded edges.
And so prepare that part of the body which is to be treated, since you have wrapped that limb or parts for the reasons required.
And when three or four days have passed, you then loosen the bandage, and you will find the bones either disjointed, broken, or dislocated, and the treatment for these conditions is as you know.
But if anyone says they are broken, then you should set them and join them as I have recommended to you in the setting of the bones of the hand. And then prepare the broken finger according to its length, and make it slightly longer; and then place this limb on the table as I have recommended to you, and wrap it with proper tightness.
But if what is broken consists of two or three or more bones, then you prepare a splint for each finger made in the quantity wrapped in linen cloth, and tighten the table at the lowest part of the limb, and let it rest on one end of the board away from the abdomen so that the tongue has good retention.
And it is important for you not to forget that you should consider all the causes mentioned to you in other fractures and accidents that we have discussed; and treat each accident with what is appropriate.
Chapter XVIII.
Of the fracture of the pubic bone in women and of the bone of the penis and the shaft of the penis in men.
When the pubic bone of a woman is broken, then perform the surgery in a reclined position, and then tilt her slightly to one side, and let her be supported behind.
By the midwife the bladder is filled with cotton, and it is made in the womb just as is hoped; and shortly after the woman is shaken, and she raises the buttocks little by little, for all this is forgotten within until it is before the exit of the womb just as is hoped and the broken bone returns.
And then place poultices on his back, and when he wants to urinate, lift the buttocks easily so that he can urinate, and reduce it and return it according to the way you mixed it at the beginning, and then return him to his original poultice.
And do this for seven days or around that time, for he will be restored then.
And if you want, take a sheep’s bladder and stretch over its neck a tube of cane; and insert the entire bladder into his wound, and then blow air through the tube vigorously until the bladder is inflated inside the wound, for the fracture will return.
And then it should be filled with cotton and kept for several days as we have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the hip joint in a man or woman is broken, administer what we have recommended for the thigh bone in its restoration and alignment.
And what is right is not hidden in these nearby fractures that rarely occur, especially for one who has studied and used this work of mine; for this fracture means to you in the major positions through straightness in its restoration and constriction, and thus you understand well.
But in the case of a man's fractured penis, take a sheath made of oxhide, and place the penis in it; then wrap it with a cloth covering, and leave it like that for three days or around that time, and it will heal.
Chapter XIX.
On the restoration of fractured bones when there is swelling.
It is convenient for him who suffers a fracture with swelling, especially if the bone is large like the femur or the humerus, or similar to these, that you prepare yourself and cut the veins of the limb immediately if the conditions for venesection indicate it, as we have said above.
And if the swelling emits blood, then you should be prepared to treat this by preventing its retention in such a way that you powder over it finely triturated substances, unless another cause is present.
And immediately begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this unless an abscess occurs.
But if an abscess occurs, then you should postpone the restoration of it until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third or fourth day, for you will cause worse accidents.
But if the broken bone protrudes above the skin, then you should reduce it and align it with your fingers as easily and with little extension as possible.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the alignment of it with your fingers is difficult, then you should reduce it using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, its length being seven or eight digits, and its width according to the size of the swelling. It is necessary for the surgeon to have three or four such instruments in different sizes suitable for various types of fractures.
And let it be round, and let there be little grease on it so that it is not doubled over when you press upon it during the operation.
And let it have a sharp point at which there is a margin in its extremities; and let the upper part be thick, and from the middle to the ends be tapered.
And this is the form of it:
And it is called in Greek "labrum voluntz atelarem," which means a small palette.
So that the end of it may be placed steady with its tip raised above the end of the prominent bone.
And press it in one motion such that the bone returns to its place, and let it be equalized with some adjustment.
And thus strive to equalize the ends of the fracture between them, and if the end of the broken bone is fine, and does not take the instrument well, then it is necessary that you incise the end of that bone so that the instrument has better access.
But if you cannot reduce the bone completely as we have recommended, then incise it with that which seems appropriate to you among the knives we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever manner is possible for you.
And smooth that which remains on the bone of roughness and small ridges.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient experiences severe pain in the tooth, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and thus do what will be of great help to the patient.
When you have completed the restoration of the bone that you are performing, and now it is summer, soak a cloth in black styptic wine and apply it properly; do not put cerot or any other substance containing oil on the wound, so that decay and corruption may not occur.
And immediately after administering plates at the time of your completion of the bone restoration, leave the wound uncovered so that you can excise any protrusions with scissors and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
And be extremely careful not to press the wound on the fracture, for many times inexperienced doctors have done this, causing their patients to suffer death or incurable illness such as gangrene. And if the swelling is slight, soft, and different from the swelling of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or it is a bad wound, and you fear some of the harmful accidents we have mentioned, and you feel pain in that area, then do not place plates on it, but make that bandage with hard cloths instead of plates and press it against it.
And after one day or two days, when the wound begins to generate granulation tissue, then you remove the cloth that you had placed on it soaked in wine, and immediately apply bandages, including those we customarily use to treat wounds, such as tetrafarmacon and similar treatments.
And it is necessary for you to untie the bandage and examine the wound every morning and evening until it is consolidated and healed, and it should be prepared in such a way that decay flows away from it to lower parts with ease.
If after many days the swelling does not consolidate and is not excised from the limb, then you should know that here one must worry about small abscesses which lie hidden within the swelling. And of those abscesses, those which are separate and can be extracted and removed, do so. For those that remain within and cause pain when the limb is moved, incise them as far as is possible for you.
And if the swelling becomes inflamed or ulcerated, which is a sign of severe corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary to proceed according to one of those methods appropriate for the treatment, as described in the preceding chapter on this subject.
And concerning the matter on which I must speak and you should pay attention: When a bone is fractured, it protrudes from the limb like the bones of the fingers and those of the small limbs, and do not attempt to pull it out or remove it because doing so may lead to death within twenty or thirty days; instead, leave it inside where it has lodged, for it will likely fall back into place on its own in that time. And then treat the swelling if you see fit, but otherwise leave it as is.
Chapter XX.
On the treatment of knots that occur as remnants of some fracture.
Often enough, this knot occurs in the remnants of a fracture during healing, especially near joints, and it hinders the normal function of the limb; and frequently prevents the limb from performing its natural movements.
Then consider whether the knot is recent and administer medicines that have a styptic effect, such as aloes, myrrh, celandine, sercapillo (celandine), and agrimony; take any of these or all of them. Prepare a poultice with one of these styptic substances or albumen from egg whites, or make it more agreeable to the patient, and apply it over the knot using linen bandages, securing it tightly in place.
There is no extraction that does not loosen the knots with words daily; and then loosen it and apply another, until the swelling subsides; or apply a strip of lead over it, for lead has the property of drawing out all that is inflamed in the limbs.
If the swelling has already become hardened into a stone and it is necessary to remove it urgently, then cut it at its most prominent part, and excise the excess and elevated portion, or grind it down with certain instruments until the swelling subsides; and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter XXI.
On the restoration of fractures when they are set and subsequently the limb is moved gently not contrary to its nature.
When a bone fracture is set, and subsequently the limb is moved gently, this is done for various reasons: among which one reason is too much loosening of the bandages and binding them not as required; or excessive constriction of the bandages preventing nourishment from reaching the limb; or excessive use of poultices; or unnecessary movement at the wrong time; or lack of blood in the patient's body and weakness.
The treatment for this is to give the patient food and make his body soft so that blood is multiplied in him; administer baths; and apply poultices and leeches to him.
And then apply a plaster to the limb, so that the plaster draws in nourishment, or continue applying poultices with warm water, until nourishment flows and returns to its natural course.
Chapter XXII.
On the treatment of broken bones when they have been set improperly and prevent their operation from what is necessary.
When it happens that the already set limb, near its healing from its curvature and prominence of the broken bone and knotting, resembles in form as if the limb were newly broken, yet the limb is not prohibited from its natural use, then there is no need for you to accept the word of one who believes that member to be freshly broken.
But now it stands as a matter of false doctors and restorers who have done this in our land; and this operation is a shameful one leading to great fear, the sign of which is fatal.
But if limping and knotting are recent, then it is necessary to wrap them in hot water containing soft herbs such as flax seeds, linseed, and melilot, and similar ones; or apply a soft plasters like well-made myrobalan paste mixed with linseed or olive oil, ground with vinegar or sour grape juice, and applied therewith; or take fatty figs and crush them with dove's milk, and similar remedies for consolidating the healing process.
And in some cases, it is dissolved through frequent gentle friction, which should be done with the most moist substances, and the limb's movement should be administered in all directions at all times.
But if the curvature has already advanced and has become hard and turned into bone, and it has reached the necessity of treatment with iron, then it is necessary to discharge the pus above it and dissolve the continuation of the bone, and carefully cut what is superfluous from the knotting of that limb with a fine instrument, and facilitate this with care and diligence.
Chapter XXIII.
On Speaking of Dislocation.
Dislocation is when any of the joints loses its proper location, thus prohibiting movement, and the figure of the limb is distorted, causing severe pain and great injuries.
When a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to immediately attempt to reduce it and not delay in doing so, because if it is delayed, swelling will occur, making the reduction of the dislocation difficult.
And for this reason, one should not move or keep the patient standing during the swelling, because it often causes spasms and severe pain.
But when this happens, you should prepare for the incision of the swollen area, and then allow it to rest until the swelling subsides. After that, bathe the limb with warm water and oil, and then reduce the dislocation gently.
And treat the entire limb with what will help it remember its proper place.
And thus I have arranged the chapters on dislocation as they were described for fractures, starting from the upper part of the body and moving to the lower parts.
Chapter XXIV.
On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Lower Jaw.
When the two jaws are dislocated, they do not delay in treatment; and the dislocation of both is done according to one of two ways: either they are moved from their places slightly and made somewhat loose; or they are completely and entirely dislocated until they become loose at the joint where saliva flows from the patient's mouth and they cannot close it, and the tongue of the patient is wrapped in a cloth.
When the dislocation of the joint is slight, it returns by itself in most cases with little cause.
If the dislocation is complete and severe, it must be reduced urgently, and should not be delayed at all.
And it is that the minister should instruct the patient to place one finger inside the mouth near the dislocated jaw if the dislocation is on one side; or both fingers together if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers should remain outside equally spaced; and order the patient to cease clenching their jaws and open them widely in each direction; and the physician should gently guide the jaw back into place until it returns to its proper position.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, then administer inhalation with hot water and oil until the reduction can be performed gently on both sides without delay, as we have said.
And when they return and are equally aligned, but the patient's mouth remains closed and not relaxed, then apply plasters made of cloth with wax on them, and make it from beeswax and oil softened; and bind it gently to facilitate movement.
And let the patient sleep on their back, with their head between two pillows, so that it does not turn to either side; and do not force them to chew for any reason; have their food be cut up finely until pain subsides and the jaws are healed, then they may eat as they wish.
With this should be administered with ease, and he should not work on the opening of the mouth for eating and drinking and speaking until the jaw is healed and cured.
And if the reduction of both jaws when they are dislocated at any time and have not returned to their places is difficult, fevers and continuous sweats often occur, and the patient's belly is easily distended, and frequently vomiting of pure bile occurs.
And when you see this, then you know that it is lost; and often the one to whom this happens dies within ten days.
Chapter XXV.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the muscle.
The collarbone is not dislocated from the chest below by its continuation to the bones; and sometimes it is dislocated outward, and this is apparent by sensation.
And the restoration of it is that the patient lies on their back and extends their arms. And then press the area with your palm with strong pressure, for it will return.
And then apply a plaster and powders and strengthen that area.
But the extremity of that which connects to the neck and is continuous with it is not dislocated on any day; therefore, it must be reduced and bandaged as we have said and as you prepare it.
And then apply a plaster, powders, and compression, and instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And this method reduces the swelling when the muscle is separated from its place.
Chapter XXVI.
On the Reduction of Muscle Dislocation.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, one of which is when it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
The second is when it is dislocated above the muscle, and this happens quickly; and it is not dislocated behind by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated in front by the nerves.
It is more often dislocated and moved out below the titillium, and especially in those whose flesh is thin, it is easily dislocated; but in those with thick flesh, it remains outside with difficulty.
