And it often happens to some men through a blow or fall that the muscle of one side becomes swollen and inflamed, and it is treated as if it were already dislocated.
Then you should treat that as if it were indeed dislocated, and proceed with its treatment accordingly.
And the dislocation is known when it is below the titillium; thus, you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the normal one, as there is a clear difference between them; you find a concavity at the head of the muscle below the titillium by touch, and the affected person cannot move their hand to their ear or make that movement with ease.
If it is dislocated, you should place the feet above, and you will immediately recognize this clearly, and it is not hidden.
And from this dislocation a quick reduction is made when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the practitioner raises the hand above to the upper parts; and then places gentle pressure below at the joint, raising it forcefully to its proper place, and the practitioner raises the hand, and extends it accordingly; and then places it gently as it returns quickly.
But if it does not return as we have said, and after the initial period when the dislocation occurred, usually several days later, then the patient should be bathed in warm water and administered a lotion to soften and relax it as if for treating rheumatism, using hyssop, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and this should be applied.
And then the patient should be placed on their back with a pillow under the joint, waiting until it is evenly relaxed between tension and restriction; and then the physician places the heel of the patient on a ball, raising the muscle upward vigorously, and pulls the patient's hand so that it is not injured below, and another assistant holds the head of the patient, so that it is not injured below, for it will immediately return to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this manner: have a person present who is taller than the patient, and stand on the side of that limb; place your muscle below the joint of the patient and raise the joint to the upper parts until the patient is suspended in the air, and another assistant pulls the patient's hand from below the navel. If the malady is stubborn, then now it must be done by one who has another cause suspended, for so the weighty one is relieved, because the dislocation returns so quickly.
And it is reduced in this other manner: it is that he be placed in the ground a pole long whose top be round in shape, as if it were a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin, and nearby be placed beneath the malady; then when so placed upon the top of the pole strong cloths, and the patient stand according to the length of the pole, and then stretch out his hand towards the sides beyond the other part; and extend the body from the other side with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if the reduction of him by all the causes we have mentioned is difficult, then use this cure: it is that you take a pole whose length be two arms, and breadth of four fingers, and the thickness of it be adjusted to the size of a thimble according to this form:
And then above the round top cloths be placed so that the pole does not hurt the patient.
And then be placed beneath the armpit of the patient, and stretch out the hand or arm over the pole towards the sides, and cloths be wrapped around the assistant and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then be placed the arm on a rung of a ladder according to its breadth, and the hand be stretched out towards the sides, and the other body be suspended from the other side, for the joint returns quickly.
And when the instrument of it is completed according to each manner that is possible to you, then place beneath the armpit a pad of wool of equal quantity; and then place an adhesive plaster prepared with powder of oak bark and egg white over all the muscle, as was mentioned above, and then bind strictly from the armpit; and the bandage be wound around the plaster, and the hand of him be attached to his neck, and leave it, and do not disturb the hand for seven days.
Let the food of the sick person be moderate until the limb is strengthened, for it is easier to achieve recovery thus.
And let the food be given more around seven or five times, and then let one exercise some movement; and thus if it is firm and not softened, now it is healed.
If the joint is dislocated due to repeated exposure to moisture that occurs to it or for some other reason, then it is necessary to administer cautery three times, as was stated before in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after seven days the bandage is removed and the joint is not stable, and the plaster and strapping are reapplied several times, but it remains unstable, and the patient cannot raise that arm above certain points, then you know that the nerves of it, which attach at the head of the muscle, have been severed or stretched or softened, and understand that the joint will never be fixed in its proper place.
Regarding the dislocation that occurs from the feet and the heel or the rear parts, the reduction is done with impulse and extension mostly, until it returns to its place.
And let the other strapping be administered to it, and care for it until it is healed.
And if during the healing process there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use baths several times until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its original nature.
Chapter XXVII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the collarbone.
Know that the joint of the collarbone is dislocated with difficulty and seemingly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, but more so towards the front than behind. And the dislocation of it is not hidden from you as it appears below the view or touch according to whatever figure it is dislocated, and when you adjust the dislocated collarbone, that is clearly declared to you, for the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm nor touch its muscle.
And you should treat it, and reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before it becomes an abscess due to cautery, for if it reaches that stage, the reduction of it is difficult, and it rarely heals completely; especially when it is in the rear parts, for it is worse than all other types of dislocations, more painful, and many people die from it.
And the restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the hand should be extended across two hands, and the arm should be expanded, and the physician's hand should be on the collarbone below it, and he should push the joint with his fingers together or with the palm of his hand until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the front parts, then it returns as he bends his hand with his other hand, until he strikes the collarbone with the palm of his hand.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer vigorous extension and strong force.
And it is that two assistants should extend the arm, and hold it firmly by both ends so that it is not moved due to the extension.
After you have thoroughly wrapped the arm, since it is covered with a long doubled cloth around the hand or with bandages.
And when the surgeon touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that this aids in the lubrication of the joint and facilitates its movement; then forcefully push the joint until it returns.
And after the reduction of the shoulder, it should rest on a support that is firm and secure on a wooden board, and it should be bound with moderate compression, and the arm should be suspended from the neck, and left like this for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and if the joint is formed in its proper place, now you loosen the bandage of it and leave it like that.
And if you see that the joint is not properly set, then you should reapply the plaster and bandages, and leave it like this for several days until it is secured; and then loosen it.
But if you encounter severe pain during the reduction of the joint and the patient is in motion, then administer hydration with baths and gentle friction, and compression until it is relieved; or have the patient pause and rest the joint, as movement exacerbates the condition; and then bandage it, and leave it like this day and night. And then lift that and place it in a bath; and when dry, you should rub the joint with moderate friction, and then reapply rest to it, as movement worsens the condition, once, and the second time, and the third time. Take an instrument for bathing, ensure that it is clean.
And if you want, place a pad over that moist and irritated fissure at the border and bind it there; do this for several applications, as this is moist and reduces to its proper condition.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the wrist, which is the joint between the arm and the hand.
Dislocation of the hand sometimes occurs, and the reduction of this dislocation is relatively easy compared to other joints; however, it is important that there be speed in reducing it when it happens, before swelling occurs or an abscess forms.
The reduction of this dislocation is done by placing the joint of the injured hand on a table and having an assistant extend the hand. The physician then places his own hand or palm over the vicinity of the joint and presses it until it returns to its proper place.
However, it must be considered whether the palm is already displaced towards the back of the hand. If that is the case, the injured person should place the two sides of their hand on a table during extension and reduction; if the dislocation is towards the back of the hand, then the position of the hand should be placed flat on the table so that the physician's hand can press directly over the joint.
And if it returns to its place immediately, that is good; but if not, then bind it with a bandage to allow for swelling and leave it like that; do not repeat this procedure, as doing so could cause pain or hinder the reduction over the days that follow.
However, the joint remains rigid according to its curvature; and do not inform the patient of any reason, except that the hand is softened and cannot grasp any cause; now you know that the nerves are severed or divided, and now there is no remedy in it but that it be strengthened with cauterization proper for the case and thus confer some relief.
Then when the dressing is removed, now pause on the bandage which we have recommended, and let it be left to rest for five days; then afterwards it should be loosened and allowed to remain loose, while the hand is exercised.
However, if the movement of it remains and you encounter any gravity in it, now treat that with hot water and friction several times, until it is alleviated.
Chapter XXIX.
On the treatment of the displacement of the said joints.
Several times dislocate the said joints completely.
And when a finger of those is dislocated towards the palm or towards the base, now press the finger and urge the dislocation with your thumbs, until it returns.
And then hold the tip of the finger and suspend it above the part to which it is dislocated, and let it remain like that for two days.
And then suspend it and extend it until it is properly bandaged on that day.
And when it is night, wrap it as described, and do not stop loosening it during the day and exercising with movement, and wrap it at night; and do this for several days until it is strong.
If it seems apparent that this is a dislocation at the elbow joint, bind it in such a way and perform with this the first operation until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated at other parts.
Chapter XXX.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation occurs to any of the vertebrae of the back or neck and they move out of their places, there is then no cure, for death is feared for the patient.
The sign of this is that the movement of the patient's body occurs without their will, and they cannot control it; often some parts of them move, such as both legs or arms, or one of them; or a vertebra is out of its place, for sometimes it moves and sometimes it returns to four directions.
When it is displaced to the rear parts, it is called kyphosis.
However, the treatment for this is to determine whether the kyphosis is due to weakness, because then there is no cure and it cannot be fully healed.
However, in the case where it is caused by falling or impact or something similar to these, the Ancients described methods of treatment at length in their discourse, from which I have condensed what is most relevant; for what little I omit is excused by my closeness to understanding and exposition, with which I have made this clear and formed it from various sources different from those used to demonstrate that and explain this.
And those are not cured except for the one that occurs specifically as I mention with this water.
And it is that the patient lie extended on his face upon a bed equal to the wall, and be laid down below the bedding moistened so that the feet of the patient are not lifted.
And near a straight post should be placed, fixed in the ground at the end of the bed around the head of the patient, and another post on the other side by the feet at the other end of the bed.
And the minister hold the post, and it should not be fixed in a hole; and another minister hold the other according to that form.
And nearby wrap around the head of the patient and below the shoulders tie a firm bandage; and extend the end of the bandage to the post which is behind the head, and attach that to it; and then stretch the other bandage over the hips of the patient with his knees, and pull up the thighs; and then adjust all the bandages and attach them to the other post which is behind the feet.
And then the minister should extend the bandage to the part of the post, and do not move the two posts and the bandages from their places in which they are fixed, but rather ensure they are not too tight as we have said.
And the physician should place his palms on the spine with strength until it returns, or place upon that a table, and then adjust the patient's feet on the table until it returns.
If this method does not return the desired result, take a table whose width or length is about three cubits and dig in the wall near the patient a place where the end of the table can fit. Then place the middle of the table on the spine, and the physician places his feet on the other end, and he should gently press until the spine is compressed and returns to its proper position.
And if you want, open again the one that is wrapped around the hand; and this is how you place the patient's head between the ends of two poles, the length of which should be one arm, and behind his head another pair of poles in the same way; and the distance between each pole should be a palm, and there should be grooves cut into each pole through which they are firmly attached to the ground so that they do not move.
And there should be a round pole in which is the wrap, and it should be wrapped around the two grooves of the two poles.
And at the end of it is a groove in which the pole is fitted, its length being one palm where it is wrapped; and on the other two poles there is something similar.
Then pull the ropes that you have tied to the patient's feet in the groove behind his head, and the ropes that you have tied to his shoulders in the wrap behind his legs.
Then behind each wrap should be a minister who turns the wrap with his hand, and the physician should adjust the spine as we have said.
And when the spine is returned to its place and the area is leveled, it is now necessary to apply an astringent poultice with egg white, and then wool; and afterwards place over the poultice a stick of wood whose width is three fingers or thereabouts, and whose length should be proportional to the extent of the swelling, and on both sides of the spine.
And then bind it with the bandage we have mentioned, and use subtle nourishment until he is healed.
And if any hardness remains in the area at the end of the healing process, it is now necessary to administer medicines that soften and smooth the affected area over a long period with the board we have recommended; and a lead plate should also be applied to that location.
And at times hardness occurs in the back of the lower spine due to dislocation; and that bone is one where it is dangerous to use this medicine, for it could easily lead to death.
Chapter XXXI.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the muscle attachment are subject only to dislocation, and do not suffer what happens to other joints with small movement and concavity.
And the joint of the hip can be dislocated in four ways, either inward, outward, forward, or backward.
And it is mainly displaced towards the interior parts, and sometimes towards the anterior or posterior parts.
The sign of the dislocation towards the interior parts is that when you adjust the thigh of the injured person next to the uninjured one, it is longer and the kneecap is more prominent than on the healthy side, and the injured person cannot bend their leg past a certain point, as this place where bending occurs is affected by swelling or cautery due to inflammation. Therefore, the head of the joint has already reached here.
The sign of the one to whom dislocation occurs towards the exterior is that the symptoms are opposite to those mentioned in this case.
The sign of the one to whom dislocation occurs towards the anterior parts is that the thigh can be raised somewhat but cannot be bent without causing pain in the knee, and if they attempt to move, they cannot do so forward; and their urine is retained, and their swallowing is affected by swelling, and they must walk on their heel.
The sign of the one to whom dislocation occurs towards the posterior parts is that they cannot raise the knee, nor can they bend it before bending the elbow, and the thigh is shorter than the other side, and the joint is softened, and the head of the joint is in that location.
However, the reduction of the various types of this dislocation is that you consider whether the dislocation is old and chronic. If it has been long-standing, you should then administer reduction according to its disposition, but at that time complete cure cannot be expected; therefore, no promise should be made regarding full recovery.
In the case where the dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four types of dislocation, proceed to rotate the joint and extend it towards both sides within and without, and move it to the right and left, for it easily returns, and no other treatment is needed.
If it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare a strong support and extend the limb from below towards the back, or along its sides, or use a bandage placed above the joint; another person should hold the hand of that limb from above in such a way as to place their hands behind the joints; and immediately apply a lion's bandage tightly over the injured area, with another person holding the extremity of the bandage.
And this extension of the limb or of the inner part towards the angle of the shoulder, or of the rear part towards the back.
And perform this extension of all those parts at once while the patient is raised off the ground and kept suspended.
And this type of extension is common to all four types.
And thus if the dislocation returns as we have said, it is good; and if not, then for every type it is necessary to proceed with what I will describe regarding its specific treatment.
In the reduction of the joint when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is towards the inner parts, have the patient lie supine on their back; then place the bandages over the injured area at the point where the head of the joint and the location behind the angle meet; and then extend the bandage from above the part of the armpit to the farthest part of the shoulder blade.
Let another strong minister take his arms, and firmly grasp the swollen part of the diseased joint and extend it outward forcefully, for that returns to its proper place.
And this method is the quickest for curing the one to which this member is reduced compared to the other methods.
But if it swells on you and does not obey the treatment with this method of cure entirely, then now it is necessary to bind both sides of the injured area tightly with a strong, quick bandage over both heels and both toes.
And let the length of each be four fingers in quantity, and let the diseased side be extended more than the other by a quantity of two fingers.
And let the diseased one be suspended above the top of the post, and hold him by his hand; and let the distance from him to the ground be two arms' lengths.
And then command a strong youth to pull on the head of the joint, and let another youth suspend the diseased one, while the other youth pulls the joint with great force, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction, when it is dislocated to the outward part, let the diseased one lie on his back as we have recommended for lying down; and bind the bandage tightly around the injured side and over his feet.
And then place two posts, one at his feet and the other at his head; and then place adjusted posts on each side of the shoulder laid upon which are wrapped wet cloths, so that the diseased one is not harmed by the post being between his arms, to prevent the extension downward from being pulled.
And each minister extends to his part, and the surgeon with his skill completes the reduction according to art.
And when the dislocation is to the front parts, then it is necessary that the patient be extended quickly, and according to this narration, he should lie on his back; and the surgeon places the palm of his right hand on the affected area, and with this compression, he extends it from the lower part towards the front.
And if the dislocation is to the rear parts, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended to the lower part, and he should be raised off the ground; however, it is necessary that he lie on a hard surface, just as it is necessary for him similarly if the shoulder joint is dislocated outward, according to what we have said about the positioning of the shoulder on two boards; and he lies on his face with the limbs extended, as we have said, there is no cure.
And it is necessary to administer compression on the table at the location of the joint.
Therefore, when the reduction is complete as it should be — and the sign of the shoulder reduction is not hidden from you, which is that you extend both arms of the patient, and when you see them equal and the patient extends his arm and expands it naturally without difficulty, then you know that the limb has returned as it should — and now you adjust two things and apply a bandage so that the shoulder is not injured in any part; and adhere to the patient resting for three days or four.
Then undo the bandage and the dressing, and compare one side with the other.
Now, if you see those marks on the skin, now you know that the dislocation has been fixed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any sign of inflammation, now you return and dress that area, and gently stretch it according to your initial structure, and let it be for three days.
Then undo that [binding], and have the patient delay in walking on each [affected area] for several days until it is strengthened.
Chapter XXXII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the joint.
The joint can be dislocated in three ways: it is dislocated towards the outer parts, towards the inner parts, and towards the lower parts more than towards the rear; but it does not dislocate towards the front.
And the sign of a dislocation is that you command the patient to apply their hand to the chest; now you know that the joint is dislocated.
And the restoration of all types of dislocation is that you command the patient to straighten it, so that they can extend their hand as much as possible; place your thumbs against the side of it, and put your feet between their feet; then press the joint with both your palms, and hold them firmly around the joint with strength, while another assistant extends their foot so that the joint returns to its proper place.
And the sign of recovery for him is that his belly adheres to his ribs without laboriousness.
And then apply a plaster to that and make the belly adhere to the ribs, and then tie those bandages with a cloth for three days or four, and then untie them.
And do not use the method of walking for several days until he is healed and comfortable.
But if the reduction of him to you is unnecessary, now administer a strong extension with bandages as described in the preceding narrative about the treatment of the hip.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the thigh.
The thigh is sometimes displaced by a slight movement, and sometimes it is dislocated completely; the dislocation of it is done either towards the interior parts or towards the exterior parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it is that you see the thigh swollen towards the part to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its displacement is easy recovery, and it should be extended with ease and equality until it returns.
But in the treatment of it when it is completely dislocated, you must perform the procedure correctly, and let him have a strong and capable assistant behind him at the middle location of it.
And then place your right hand on the uppermost part of the thigh, and your left hand below the knee at the location of the joint.
Take the leg and straighten it towards your right side, then pull it with both hands towards your left side, and extend it to the left; and press down with your feet on the leg towards your right side as you work.
Do this twice as we have counted.
Then press down with your feet on the leg for the third time while you are at the nail.
Then return it in one attempt or two according to this method, and if you find the leg equalized, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation immediately as it returns.
But if it is forbidden for you to reduce the leg as we have recommended, then lay the patient on their back on the ground, and fix a board firmly on the ground under the injured part, and place it between the bones, and wrap the area in cloth so that you do not displace the injury.
Then have one assistant hold the patient's limbs, and another assistant extend the leg with his hands or with bandages around the ankle; and then each assistant should pull against the extension on their side, and the board should be placed between the bones of the injury holding it in place so that the body is not pulled away from the area during the extension.
Then the physician should realign the dislocation with his hands, and another assistant hold the healthy limb on the opposite side, as the dislocation will return to its place with ease.
And when the dislocation returns and you are sure of the successful reduction, now apply a plaster and firmly bandage it with strong bandages, and bind the leg to the board with bandages.
Be careful and guard against the nerves which are behind on the heel, so that the ligature upon them is not violent and weakens them greatly.
And apply that for two days or three; and if the ligature has softened, remove it.
And untie that on the third day or on the fourth, and prohibit the patient from moving for forty days. But if it is necessary for them to move before that time, there is no guarantee that the displacement will not dissolve and corrupt, and therefore it will not be receptive to treatment.
And if it happens that the abscess needs to be cauterized, you should then administer according to the guidance of the liquid in which our narration proceeds, in another place for treatment and application until it is healed.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the treatment of dislocation of the said foot bones.
Ensure that the one who is dislocated rests with some extension without work; and what is not difficult is easily accomplished.
But if the dislocation is in some joints of the two feet, then you should have the patient lie on a flat surface on the ground or on a table, and let them be positioned as if they were walking.
And then you stand on one foot, and place your foot on the area that needs to be reduced, and then step on it firmly until it returns to its proper position, so that it appears level and no longer protrudes.
After the pause, place the foot on a board that will support the entire foot, which has two ends, and then strengthen it with a proper and firm structure for three days; then loosen that and allow it to rest from walking for ten days, until it is strengthened and its improvement is assured.
Chapter XXXV.
About the types of dislocation that are caused by injury, fracture, or any such circumstances.
When one of those causes occurs, and you fail in the treatment of it and in its restoration, many times death results.
And because of this, one should not presume to treat it unless they are wise in the art, experienced over long use, merciful and compassionate, neither hasty nor presumptuous; and at the beginning, let them use medicines that only reduce swellings, and leave the patient's fate to divine judgment, but seek help from someone to whom you hope for salvation from destruction, or relief from illness, in whom there appears some hope. And then strive to perform the reduction for them as soon as possible at the beginning of the cause, before it becomes a swollen abscess.
And then if the limb returns as we want it to, immediately administer a regimen that reduces swelling and treats the injury with what is appropriate from the available dry remedies.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and occurs in the separated bones, then you must treat the condition as we have said for simple injuries, dealing with it as has been described for such places.
And guard and avoid according to your virtue that of the instrument in the ways of fear as it has been preceded by my preaching; for this is better for you to serve your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and most wholesome for your true body you will see these in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis perfectly, for which God be blessed. Amen.
